order.


Word has this day been received from His Majesty's Ship Goliath, a storied
and respected seventy-four gunner, presently cruising to the southward. Her
captain requests the transfer of Sergeant Rowan Maddox of the Royal
Marines, whose conduct in the late action with the brigantine Fury has been
reported with distinction.


It is a satisfaction to forward such a man. Posting to a third-rate ship of
the line is a distinct honour few Marines attain at his stage of service,
and I do not doubt he will rise swiftly amongst that company.


In consequence of his departure, Corporal Garrick Broome is hereby promoted
to Sergeant, effective at the next watch. No man aboard is better suited to
the charge.


As the regulations of Goliath forbid animals borne on strength, Maddox has
requested that the dog Joss remain aboard Kingfisher. I have consented, for
the creature is well regarded by officers and men alike and has shown no
small attachment to Broome.


A rendezvous with HMS Goliath has been agreed upon for tomorrow's forenoon
tide, weather permitting, at which time the transfer will be made in proper
form.


-- Capt. William Hale, R.N.


* * *


The first man to see her was the masthead lookout, but he scarcely needed
to call. Sometimes word runs faster along a deck than any voice from aloft.


By the time the hail came down, half the watch already gazed eastward. I
shaded my eyes with one hand and saw her then, a pale giant rising out of
the morning haze, canvas like tiered cliffs and a hull that seemed to stand
upon the sea rather than float in it.


"God's bones," Barnes breathed beside me. "There she is."


Croft narrowed his eyes, following the line of her masts.


"Seventy-four guns," he said. "Look at the fall of her tops. That'll be
Goliath, all right."


I had heard the name in the berthing cabin the night before, when the news
of Maddox's promotion went round, but hearing it now with that ship before
us was another thing entirely. Kingfisher had felt like a stout little
world to me until that moment. Next to Goliath she might have passed for a
ship's launch.


Her masts seemed to scrape the sky. Three great tiers of guns peered from
her sides, the lower ports so near the water they flashed dark and bright
as she rolled. The black and yellow of her paint ran in long bands, warlike
and handsome. Men lined her rail already, a thin fringe of scarlet and
blue, watching us with the careless air of those who know their own weight.


The talk along our deck took on a different sound. Pride and envy, both.


"Look at the size of her anchors," someone said.


"You could house a family in one of them gunports," another replied.


Mr Merrick had his glass out, conferring with Captain Hale in low
tones. Orders went from them to the quartermaster, and Kingfisher answered
the helm delicately, trimming her head so the big ship lay off our weather
bow. The sea between us shivered with the change of wake.


"They'll send a boat," Croft said. "Be ready to dress the rail, Kit. Mind
you keep your hands off your hat unless the captain does it first, or
Merrick'll have your hide."


"I know how to stand still," I said.


He gave me a sideways look that said I did not always, but the slight grin
neutered it as a disciplinary tool.


The hail came from Goliath's quarterdeck through a speaking trumpet, formal
and clear. Hale answered in the same fashion. Flags went up, dipped,
answered. Whatever had been agreed upon in letters now took shape in canvas
and hull and measured distance.


"Boat away!" someone cried from the great ship.


We watched as a cutter lowered from her davits, swinging free, then touched
water with a soft splash that carried even across that stretch of sea. Oars
were shipped; a sergeant's voice barked the stroke. The little craft leapt
forward, white flashes at each pull.


Along Kingfisher's gangway the hands fell into order. Merrick called for
the bosun's mates. Pipes shrilled as the side-party mustered; two seamen,
one marine, all turned out as clean as a man could be after weeks at
sea. There was a murmur further aft where last night's cheer for Maddox
still hung in the imaginations of the men. No grief as such; rather a sense
of having seen a comrade rise in station, and wondering what shape the deck
would take without him.


The cutter came smartly alongside us. Lines were tossed, hooked on. The
officer in the cutter's stern rose, his coat brushed, sword at his hip, and
reached for our ladder. He came up first, saluted Captain Hale, and gave
his name and rank.


"Lieutenant Harford, sir, of His Majesty's Ship Goliath."


"Welcome aboard, Mr Harford," Hale replied, touching his hat. "You bring us
good men, I trust."


"The very best we could spare, sir," Harford said, with the faintest
flicker of humour. "And one you may wish we had kept."


That earned him a few hidden smiles along the rail. He stepped aside to let
the transfers come up.


Two marines first. The elder had the look of an old hand: square jaw, face
marked by old weather more than old blows, gray at the temples. The younger
shifted his weight from foot to foot, jaw clenched a shade too tight, eyes
drinking in the smaller ship with a mixture of curiosity and unease. Behind
them came a drummer-lad with a scarlet coat that did not yet fit his
shoulders, clutching his sticks as if he thought someone might take them
from him.


Broome watched them from his post by the mainmast. His new sash lay folded
over one arm for the present. When Maddox stepped forward to meet the
arrivals, the men around us hushed a little.


"Sergeant Maddox," Harford said. "This is Corporal Briggs, Private Ellis,
and Drummer Shaw, assigned to Kingfisher by order of the colonel of marines
aboard Goliath."


Maddox shook each man's hand in turn, his grip firm and brief. When he came
to the drummer, his stern face softened a fraction.


"Play clean, listen sharp, and you'll do," he said. "Broome will take you
in hand."


At the sound of his name, Broome moved up, drawing a breath that seemed to
set his shoulders wider. The men parted for him without thinking. Last
night they had cried his name along with Maddox's in the lower deck; this
morning made it official.


Maddox turned to him and unfolded the sash. For a moment they stood close,
the old scarlet resting across both their hands. Joss, who had lain under
the carronade watching all this with solemn eyes, rose and pressed his head
into Maddox's thigh with a soft sound.


"Hold still, you great lump," Maddox murmured to the dog, though his hand
found the broad head and rubbed behind one ear as he spoke.


Then he faced Broome squarely.


"You've been doing the work these past months," he said, low enough that
only those nearest heard. "This only puts it where all can see."


Broome's throat worked once.


"I learned under a good man," he answered.


Maddox's mouth twitched, almost a smile. "Make a better one," he said, and
laid the sash across Broome's chest, drawing it round and fastening it off
with practised fingers.


There was a quiet exhale from the marines around them, a shifting of weight
that carried more meaning than any cheer. Joss stepped back, then moved in
again, this time leaning his great head against Broome's hip. Broome's hand
dropped to the dog's neck as naturally as breath.


"All right," Maddox said softly to Joss. "You've picked your sergeant."


I felt something warm behind my ribs at that. Promotion was one thing in a
captain's neat hand; quite another in the lay of cloth and the way a dog
chose where to rest.


Lieutenant Harford cleared his throat.


"And lastly," he said, a glint in his eye as if he had saved something for
effect, "Able Seaman Thomas Quick, late of Goliath's fore top."


Quick came up the side as if the ladder belonged to him.


He moved with an easy balance that marked a man long at sea. Dark hair tied
back with a tarred string, shirt open a button more than regulation, neck
browned, eyes quick and amused. He let his gaze run along Kingfisher's deck
in one long sweep before he saluted Captain Hale.


"Sir," he said. His voice had a London tumble to it, though it was polished
by years among men from all over the kingdom. "Begging your leave to join
your company."


"You have it," Hale replied. "Keep your hands to their work and your tongue
within bounds, and we shall suit one another well enough."


"I'll do my best on both counts, sir," Quick said. It was impossible to
tell which he meant more.


As he stepped back from the captain, his eyes flicked across the gathered
hands again. They lingered a moment on Croft, taking in the breadth of him,
the easy way he stood. Then they came to me, and his smile deepened by some
private degree.


"Friendly-looking vessel, this," he remarked aloud. "After a seventy-four,
I feel half naked. Might do a man good."


"That depends what you are used to baring," Barnes muttered at my shoulder.


Quick heard him. His gaze slid to Barnes, weighing him, then to Broome in
his new sash.


"Sergeant," he said with a little inclination of his head. "You'll be the
one standing there if I stray."


"Correct, seaman," Broome replied. His tone was calm; there was no threat
in it, only fact. "And you'll find there is less room to get lost on a
sloop than there is on a 3rd rate ship of the line."


Quick's mouth curved.


"Aye," he said. "But fewer eyes to see when a man does anything worth
seeing."


That drew a smothered cough of amusement from somewhere behind us. Mr
Merrick's glance swept that way like a knife, and the sound died.


Hale and Harford withdrew a few paces to finish their formal
business. There were papers to sign, signals to confirm, arrangements for
ammunition and dispatches. To us along the rail, the great weight of it
turned on smaller things. Which hammock would hang where. Whose mess would
take the newcomer. How the line of marines would look tomorrow with a
different figure at their head.


Maddox moved among his old men one last time, speaking to each in turn. No
speeches, no embraces. A hand to a shoulder here. A few quiet words
there. When he came to Croft he paused an extra heartbeat.


"You keep that boy from doing anything heroic without warning you first,"
he said, with a flick of his eyes toward me.


"I'll try," Croft answered. "He's grown a mind of his own, though."


"I've noticed," Maddox said. His gaze shifted to me. "You see to it you
don't make a liar of him, Kit."


"I'll do my duty, Sergeant," I said.


"I expect you'll do more than that," he replied, and there was a warmth in
it that made my face burn.


When at last he went down the side into Goliath's cutter, Joss paced along
the rail, head low, tail moving in restless sweeps. At the gangway the dog
stopped, planting himself as if he meant to bar the way.


"Joss," Maddox said quietly, looking up from the boat. "Stay."


The word dropped into the stillness between ships.


For a breath the dog quivered, torn between two worlds. Then he turned and
walked back to Broome's side, pressing against his leg with a soft,
resigned sigh. Something passed over Broome's face that I could not name,
only feel.


Maddox gave a small nod, as if that choice had been the one he had most
feared and most hoped for, all at once.


"Look after them," he called up.


"I will," Broome said.


The cutter shoved off. Oars dipped and rose in smooth rhythm, carrying him
away toward the looming side of Goliath. As he drew near her entry port, a
boatswain's pipe rose clear and thin. Marines lined her gangway for him,
red coats bright against the dark flank of the great hull. He mounted her
steps without looking back until the last moment. Then, hand on the entry
rail, he turned once and lifted his hat toward us.


Along Kingfisher's deck, hats and hands answered him, a small forest of
movement against our own low rail.


"Best of luck, Sergeant," Barnes said under his breath.


"Aye," Croft murmured. "He'll make his mark with that crew soon enough."


Quick had been watching the whole while, expression unreadable. When Maddox
vanished among the figures on Goliath's deck, Quick let out a slow breath.


"So," he said softly, more to himself than anyone. "That's the man they've
traded me for."


He glanced sideways at Broome, at the set of his shoulders, the dog at his
heel, the marines flanking him a little more stiffly than the day before.


"Seems I have work to do," Quick added, and there was something like
admiration under the lazy tone.


"You've hammocks to learn first," Croft said, coming up beside him. "Then
Kingfisher's ways. Leave the work to Broome for now. He knows them."


Quick looked him over, taking his measure. "And you are?"


"Croft."


"Ah. The one from the fore top I was warned about." Quick smiled. "They
said there was a fellow aboard here it would be a pleasure to vex."


"You'll find no shortage of men willing to try," Croft answered mildly.


Quick's gaze flicked to me once more.


"And you'd be Kit," he said. "Our little hero of the Fury."


My cheeks warmed again before I could stop them.


"I was in the right place when the shot flew," I said. "That is all."


"Sometimes," Quick said, "that is everything. Rumors flew on Goliath. We
may have been a big ship, but a smart eagle knows when to admire the
sparrow."


He gave me a wink, then turned to follow Merrick toward the hatchway, his
kit over one shoulder as if it weighed nothing at all.


The shadow of Goliath lay across our deck a little longer, then drifted as
her helm went over. Soon enough she would fill her canvas and move off on
whatever course the Admiralty had marked for her. Kingfisher would have her
own orders to follow.


But for the space of that morning, with Joss pressed to Broome's side,
Croft at my shoulder, and Quick's laugh still hanging faint in the air, I
felt the shape of our small world change. The loss of one man. The gain of
another. New weight on old timbers.


The sea, indifferent, lay bright and flat around us, as if nothing at all
had altered.


* * *


By the time Kingfisher's canvas filled again and the Goliath had fallen
astern, the talk on our own deck turned inward. The officers finished with
their courtesies, the last signals were hauled down, and the order came
sharp and plain:


"Hands below. Find these new lambs a rack."


The word went through the lower deck like a stone dropped in a pond. Men
shifted, made space, grumbled and joked in the same breath. You could feel
the ship drawing herself close again, folding all that spectacle from the
seventy-four back into the cramped, salt-rich world we knew.


I went down with Croft and Barnes, ducking the beams as we dropped into the
berth. The heat met us first, held down by the deckhead: lantern-warm,
thick with sweat and tar and the day's labour. Hammocks were still triced
up along the beams, leaving a clear run of space for men and chests.


"Make room, you idle lot," Barnes called, good-natured. "You'd think we'd
never taken fresh hands aboard before."


"You're only sour," Sutter said from his chest by the mast, "because the
new pretty one's a topman and not a gunner."


"Who says I wanted him?" Barnes shot back.


Croft gave him a look that did not need words. Barnes laughed into his
hand.


Quick came down last of the transferred seamen, swinging lightly from
ladder to deck as if the whole ship were made for his feet. His kitbag
thumped against his shoulder. He took one slow turn, seeing the space as a
man sizes a room he means to live in.


"This your nest, then?" he said. "Cozy."


"You've the gunner's berth," said the bosun's mate, jerking his chin toward
our mess. "There, between young Kit and Sutter. Hammock's stowed above
you. Stow your gear sharpish and keep clear of the pump-well. You foul it,
you clean it."


"Aye, sir," Quick said easily; then, catching sight of Croft, added under
his breath, "Could be worse, then."


He slid his bag to the deck beside the empty space among our chests. The
canvas had seen better years, patched at the corners, but his hands were
quick and neat as he loosed the ties.


I tried not to stare, and failed.


Up close, Thomas Quick was better than I expected.


He set his bag down and bent to it, dark hair tied back at his nape, a few
loose strands slipping free as he worked. The lantern light caught along
his cheek and jaw, showing a short growth of beard there, dark and even,
the sort that looked kept without fuss. His face was open, shaped by sun
and salt more than age, and when he glanced up I caught the flash of green
in his eyes. Bright eyes. Quick eyes. They seemed to take in the whole deck
at once and still have room left for amusement.


He was built the way a sailor ought to be built. Broad through the
shoulders, narrow at the hips, his arms thick with rope work muscle under
rolled sleeves. Nothing polished about him. Nothing stiff. Just a body that
knew its work and moved easily within it. When he shifted his weight, the
deck answered him as if it recognized the balance.


Then he smiled.


It came fast and easy, a crooked grin that started at one corner of his
mouth and spread, warm and unguarded. It did something to the space around
him. The air felt lighter for it. I realized, watching him stow his things
with an unhurried confidence, that Quick was the kind of man people liked
having close in a place like this. Handsome without trying. Strong without
posturing. The sort who'd make a crowded berth feel less cramped, simply by
being himself.


I looked away before he caught me staring, but the impression lingered,
bright as the lantern light on his eyes.


A seaman's duffel tells his story more plainly than his mouth ever
will. Out came a folded shirt, finer linen than most of us carried; a pair
of extra slops, worn but well-mended; a short length of tarred line,
whipped at both ends with red thread instead of plain; a small wooden box
no bigger than a man's hand, which he turned away from us a little as he
set it aside.


Barnes saw that and raised one brow.


"Got a sweetheart in there?" he said. "We don't allow wives below."


Quick smiled without showing teeth.


"Just combs and miracles," he replied. "Can't have the King's rigging
looking dull, can we?"


He plucked the box open with his thumb. Inside were indeed a comb, a scrap
of cloth that caught a faint scent of bay when the air stirred it, and a
tiny tin with some dark salve worked to shine. He touched his hair as if in
explanation.


"Goliath breeds them vain," Sutter muttered, loud enough to carry.


"Goliath had officers," Quick said mildly, "who liked their topmen to look
as if they might be trusted in a gale, not hauled out of a hog-pen."


Barnes laughed, delighted. "Listen to him. First hour aboard and he's
correcting our manners."


Quick glanced my way, eyes glinting in the half-light. He tilted his head,
studying my cheek as if to check the surgeon's work, then seemed satisfied
and turned to lash his hammock lanyard to the beam hook above his new
place.


"You've his height near enough," he said to Croft as he worked. "Easier for
the eye, that."


Croft looked up from the knife he was whetting.


"You want measuring for anything beyond a hammock," he said, "you give the
sergeant notice first."


Quick only smiled, unfazed.


"Aye, I see how it is."


Broome entered then, coming across from the marines' corner when one of his
men nodded that they were settled. He stopped by Quick's chest and looked
down at it, then at the man himself.


"You've your place?" he said.


"Aye, Sergeant."


"You've your duty hours?"


"Aye."


Broome's gaze flicked to the little box with its comb and scent-cloth still
open. One of his brows quirked, the barest fraction.


"We rise to the pipes, not to the glass," he said. "Keep your elbows in. Do
your work. You'll find the ship kindly enough."


"Yes, Sergeant," Quick said. He had the tone right; respectful, but with
that thread of humour that men like Broome watch for and weigh.


Broome's eye lingered one moment longer, as if he were marking Quick for
later consideration, then he turned away. Joss hauled himself to his feet
and followed, nails clicking lightly on the planks.


"Do you polish the dog as well?" Quick murmured, just loud enough for our
mess to hear.


Barnes choked back a laugh. Sutter shook his head.


"You'll have your hide tanned before the week is out," he said.


"That would require I give him my back," Quick replied, untroubled. "I've
no habit of that."


Croft slid his knife back into its sheath with a soft, final sound.


"You've a long tongue, Quick," he said. "Mind you don't bite it."


Quick looked at him properly for the first time with that measuring calm
again. If he felt the weight of Croft's gaze, he did not show it.


"Would you care to mind it for me?" he said lightly. "You look as if you've
the knack."


Barnes made a small, strangled sound.


I busied myself with my own chest, though I could feel a strange brightness
under my ribs, as if someone had set a lantern behind them. There was no
malice in Quick's manner. Only mischief, and a kind of reckless charm I
could see men falling in with too quickly if they weren't careful.


Croft's expression did not change at first, but after a few seconds, he had
the good grace to grin.


"I mind the ship," he said. "Sergeant minds you. You mind yourself."


Quick inclined his head, as if accepting an agreed division of labour.


"As you please," he said. "I've no wish to spoil your snug little kingdom."


"Kingfisher's no kingdom," Barnes put in. "She's a family. That's worse."


"Families quarrel," Quick said. "Makes for better stories."


He tugged on his hammock, testing the line. Satisfied, he stepped back and
gave the swaying canvas a light tap with his knuckles.


"There," he said. "I've seen worse nests. On Goliath we were stacked three
deep in some corners."


"We're none of us as grand as that," Sutter said. "But we turn out handsome
enough when called."


"You can show me how," Quick answered. "I'll pay careful heed."


His glance strayed to me at that, quick as his name. Something in it made
my mouth dry again.


Barnes saw that too. His grin grew slow and lazy.


"Careful, Kit," he said. "He'll have your shirt off and be borrowing your
blankets if you look at him with those eyes."


I felt the heat rise in my face and bent lower over the buckle I was
adjusting.


"I look at everyone the same," I said.


"That is your difficulty," Croft remarked.


He said it with such calm that the mess laughed, easing the charge a
little. Quick's smile softened, as if he'd found a tune he liked.


"I won't poach," he said toward Croft, tone almost peaceable. "On Goliath
we had a rule: first man to mark his berth keeps it."


Croft's jaw ticked, despite himself.


"You talk too much of Goliath," he said. "You're on Kingfisher now. Learn
her ways instead."


"I mean to," Quick replied. "That's half the reason I begged the captain's
leave. The other half was that I heard you lot make your own amusements."


Barnes' eyes lit.


"Who told you that?"


"Marines gossip worse than washerwomen," Quick said. "One of Goliath's lads
bares his teeth if you so much as share a blanket. Said Kingfisher's
another matter." His gaze slid once more over our close-hung hammocks, the
narrow run of deck between them, Joss already curling himself at Broome's
boots among the marines. "I see he was not wrong."


There was a murmur at that, low and pleased in some throats, wary in
others. Sutter gave Quick a frankly speculative look. Broome did not turn
his head, but I saw the way his fingers stilled briefly on the strap he was
tightening.


Croft's eyes found mine. There was a mild warning in them, and something
else besides, a curiosity of his own, unfriendly to no man, but protective
of his ground.


"Kingfisher has her customs," he said. "I daresay you'll hear of them soon
enough."


Quick held his gaze for a few heartbeats, then he dipped his chin.


"As you say."


The bosun's call trilled overhead, sharp and thin through the beams.


"Hands to supper!"


Men began to move, hauling hammocks down to hang for the night, making way
for the mess table boards to be set up along the keel. The familiar,
comforting disorder of the hour spread surely through the berth: the
clatter of pewter, the scrape of bench legs, the rich smell of salt meat
and peas as the kids came down from the galley.


Quick stepped back to give room for the table, then leaned close enough to
murmur to me, voice low and amused:


"Do you always let him speak for you?"


I swallowed.


"He doesn't," I said. "He speaks for the ship."


"And you?" Quick's gaze dipped to my mouth for the briefest instant. "Who
speaks for you?"


"I do," I said.


Croft laid a ladle of stew into my bowl then, his arm briefly cutting
between us. Whether by chance or no, I could not tell.


"Eat," he said. "You talk too much on an empty stomach."


"Yes, Tom," Quick said lightly.


Croft's eyes narrowed at the use of his name. Barnes hid another grin
behind his spoon.


We ate, then, the way men always do at sea; quickly, noisily, with little
time for refinements. Yet the talk around the board bore the new weight of
change. The marines from Goliath and Kingfisher's own men mixed with
cautious civility. Joss thumped his tail under the table at the smell of
gristle. One of the new lads from the line asked Sutter what work there was
aloft; another wanted to know how often we saw land.


Quick listened more than he spoke once he had gauged the temper of the
mess. When he did offer a story, it was trimmed to the right length and
salted with enough danger to hold attention; a gale in the Bay under
close-reefed topsails, a man washed from the forechains and clawed back by
his hair. He had the gift for it. I could feel the men joining him in those
remembered seas, making room for him in their minds.


Yet even as the noise grew, he never quite stopped watching. His eyes
ranged the berth, weighing Broome's quiet, and Barnes' loose-limbed ease,
Sutter's quick tongue.


I felt his glance on Croft and me more than once, a natural sort of
interest, as if he had come from a colder world and found the warmth here
curious.


When supper was done and the tables stowed, men swung up into their
hammocks, the space drawing close around us. The lanterns were turned down
to a softer glow. The familiar chorus of coughs, mutters, and shuffling
limbs took hold. Quick disappeared above, to take his watch.


I lay back with my hands folded behind my head, watching the smoke marks on
the beams fade as my eyes adjusted. Croft's hammock rocked gently beside
mine, his arm hanging loose over the side. I could have reached over and
curled my fingers around his wrist if I pleased.


He reached over, and his fingers brushed my shoulder in passing as he
resettled. The touch was light, unremarked by anyone else.


Kingfisher creaked and sighed around us, planks working gently in the night
swell. I imagined that Goliath's lanterns were only the faintest smear now,
somewhere beyond the hull. Our world was once more this low-roofed cavern
of wood and rope and breath, with one more heart beating among us.


I lay awake longer than usual, listening to that new rhythm slide itself
quietly into the ship's own.


* * *


The hours passed.


The lanterns had been hooded long since, leaving only a low amber glow
along the beams: enough to find one's hammock, not enough to read a face
unless it turned to the light. The heat still clung to the planks from the
day's sun, and the air had that closeness it takes when a hundred men
breathe the same hour.


Croft had come to my hammock with me, as he did at times when we wished to
speak quietly before sleep. He settled behind me, one arm loosely around my
waist, his chest warm against my back. His breath was slow, the kind that
comes just before true rest. I could feel him drifting, the weight of him
softening, his chin brushing my shoulder now and again as the hammock
swayed.


Around us, the lower deck quieted by degrees. A cough here, a muttered oath
as someone shifted position, a ripple of canvas. Then the hush thickened.


That was when Quick came in. He had been topside for the last watch, and
the salt still shone faintly along his arms and throat. He moved through
the berth as if the ship herself were watching him; easy, unhurried, bare
feet silent on the planks. Men stirred at his passing but did not fully
wake.


He reached his own hammock, directly opposite mine.


I felt Croft breathe against my back, felt his arm across my belly.


Quick faced us as he slid his shirt and slops off, then he turned toward
the hanging bed.


The canvas of the hammock dipped as he lowered himself in, one knee braced,
shoulders rolling back. The lantern's dim flare caught the line of him: the
sheen of sweat on his ribs, the taut curve of his abdomen, the long stretch
of muscle as he lifted his arms to settle the lashing above his head.


He knew I was looking. I knew it because of the slow, lazy grin that spread
across his handsome face.


His hands ran slowly down his torso, smoothing the salt from his skin as
though easing some ache left from the day's work. The hammock shifted with
him, fabric creaking softly. There was nothing hurried in the motion, but
nothing timid either. It was the way a man touches himself when he is proud
of his body and knows his audience.


Heat rose in my face. I swallowed and tried to look toward the
deckhead. Croft's arm moved against my abdomen, only sleepily aware of my
own movements. He was not fully awake, but sleep had loosened him; he
breathed against my neck, steady, unthreatened.


I stared across the gap, my eyes half-lidded and hungry. I couldn't help
it.


Quick was watching me openly now, the faintest smile on his lips, wicked as
any I had seen, and entirely sure of itself. His hand moved lower on his

Captain's Log -- HMS Kingfisher


8 February 1790. In latitude 13° 40' N, longitude 62° 15' W.
Wind E by S, moderate. Weather fair.


Having quitted Bridgetown four days past with fresh water, beef, biscuit
and naval stores sufficient for six weeks, His Majesty's sloop Kingfisher
continues her passage westward along the Lesser Antilles, maintaining sound
condition in hull and rig. Soundings regular.


On the 6th instant we encountered the brigantine sighted at dawn, bearing
WSW on a light breeze. She proved hostile; her colours were struck late in
the engagement. The enemy mounted eight guns to our sixteen and was carried
after thirty minutes' close action. Casualties aboard Kingfisher remain
light: one marine dead, three seamen wounded, and several contusions among
the hands from splinters and shot. The brig's survivors, twelve in number,
were taken prisoner and her cargo inventoried for Admiralty records.


The conduct of the crew in the action was commendable, particularly the
rigging party under Able Seaman Croft, who kept the foretop secure under
sustained fire. Young Kittridge, landsman, behaved with notable steadiness
for a new hand and has earned mention to the Lieutenants for good order.


Our present intent is to make port at Basseterre, depending on wind and
water, to take on fresh provisions and deliver dispatches carried from the
Barbados station. The men stand in good spirits. No desertion
feared. Expect to grant leave in rotation upon anchoring.


~ Edmund Hale, Captain.

"I love you too, Croft."  I smile at him, at the deep blue of his
eyes. "What happens now?"


He stepped closer, close enough that his breath warmed my lips.


"Now?" He let his hand fall to the back of my neck, his thumb resting just
under the hairline. "Now you're ours. The men's... and mine."


He drew me in, forehead against mine. For a moment neither of us moved; the
ship creaked softly beneath us, the sea lapped at the bow, and the world
felt very small, made only of breath and wood and the faint scent of tar
and him.


His fingers slid down my throat, resting over the pulse he'd learned too
well.


"You'll sleep by me tonight?"


I nodded.


He reached down to take up his dropped coat, and wrapped it around my
shoulders. It was still warm from his body. I leaned into him without
thinking.


He guided me down into the soft dark of the lower deck, toward our hammock,
toward whatever the night meant for us now, bodies worn out, hearts alight.


The sea rocked us slowly to sleep.


* * *

Untitled (Chapter 5)

Date: Wed, 3 Dec 2025 06:56:45 -0800
From: J Wize

* * *


The sea looked different that morning.


I could not have said exactly why at first. The sky was clear enough, the
wind fair, the swell no worse than any other day since we had left
harbour. Yet there was something about the light that set my teeth on edge,
as if the sun had hung back a little from the world and was watching us
from behind a cloud instead.


The change began, as most things did, with the officers' faces. Men who
joked on fine clear mornings did not joke now. Orders went along the deck
in low voices. Hale's spyglass was out more often than usual, and
Mr. Merrick seemed to live at the rail, his eyes drawn again and again to
the eastern horizon.


Croft had felt it before I did. I woke to find him already out of the
hammock, shirt thrown over his head, hair still wet from water he'd thrown
through it in haste. The rest of the berth stirred with the grumbling
sounds of watch change.


His jaw was set in a way I had learned to recognise; a sort of braced calm
a man puts on when there is work coming that he would rather not do.


"You're early," I said, pushing myself up on one elbow.


He glanced over his shoulder. In the half-light below, his eyes looked
almost colourless.


"Rouse yourself," he said quietly. "They'll want all hands smartly." Then,
after a heartbeat. One of the lookouts spotted a sail, and the sky is an
ill omen this morn."


That drew me out of the hammock faster than any order. By the time I had my
shirt on and my slops tied, the talk in the berth had shifted. Barnes swung
his legs down, rubbing at his face with one broad hand.


"What's the shout?" he muttered. "Are we taking on more water or more
trouble?"


"Trouble moves quicker," another man said.


There was nervous laughter at that, but it died away as soon as it
started. Above us we could hear the thud of feet and the clatter of gear
being shifted from its peacetime place. Somewhere forward, a voice called
for the deck to be cleared. Joss barked once, short and sharp, then was
hushed.


When we went up, the air tasted different, sharper, edged with powder that
had not yet been fired but was being made ready. Men were already moving
the guns, running them out a little so the trucks sat easy, lashing down
anything that might become a missile if the ship lurched under fire.


I looked instinctively for Captain Hale. He stood near the quarterdeck
rail, hat tucked beneath his arm, glass to his eye. His posture alone told
the tale. There was no sign of alarm in it, but a gathering of force, like
a line drawn taut.


Mr. Merrick stood not far from him, conferring with the ship's
lieutenant. Their voices did not carry, but their motions did; short,
contained, purposeful. No one gestured wildly. It was the quiet that filled
my veins with ice. It was... different from our normal noise.


Barnes came up beside me, adjusting the fall of his neckerchief.


"Eyes front, lad," he said, not unkindly. "If there's anything to see, the
captain will tell you soon enough."


"I feel as if I ought to see it already," I said.


He gave me a sidelong look.


"You will," he replied. "Sooner than you like."


Croft had gone forward with another party to help clear the waist. When I
looked that way, I saw him stripping loose coils of line from the bitts and
sending them below, stowing anything that might trip a man at the wrong
moment. His movements were economical, precise. Once he straightened and
saw me watching; our eyes met for a breath. He tipped his chin, the
smallest of acknowledgements, then turned back to his work.


The call came not long after.


"All hands on deck!" The bosun's pipe shrilled over the deck, the note
rising and falling with a sickening familiarity I had heard only in
exercise till now. "All hands!"


The words fell like stones into the space between beats of my heart. Men
who had been moving in twos and threes now broke away to their places, and
the loose murmur of talk vanished. The marines formed up smartly near the
mainmast, their red coats stark against the pale wood, muskets at the
ready. Broome's face looked as if it had been carved out of something
harder than flesh. Maddox moved among his men with a low word here and
there, settling them.


I went to my station at the third port gun, hands already sweating. We had
practiced this often enough; passing powder, fetching shot and wadding,
keeping clear of the recoil when the piece went off. In drills, there had
always been an air of weary humour about it. Now the same movements felt as
if the ship itself were holding its breath with us.


"Keep your head, Kit," the gun captain said. "You know the work. Mind your
fingers round the trucks, and keep your ears open. You'll do fine."


"Aye," I answered, and surprised myself that my voice came out even.


A bell rang aft. Not the watch bell, but a single note, clear and
unwavering.


Hale lowered his glass.


"Mr. Merrick," he called, voice clear over the wind. "Beat to quarters, if
you please."


The drum began in the waist, a low, insistent rattle that walked down my
spine. It spoke to some animal part of me that had little to do with words;
go, stand, hold fast. My mouth had gone dry.


Curiosity still caught me, even then. Between orders, when I could spare a
glance, I looked out past our gunport, squinting against the glare.


At first it was nothing more than a smudge on the horizon, darker than the
sea, not yet fully a shape. Then the smudge took on spars, a line of
canvas, the pale glint of water breaking at the bow. A brig, by the look of
her, someone said. She rode with a swagger I did not like, as if she knew
exactly what she wanted and meant to have it.


"She's no friend," the gun captain muttered. "Look at the cut of her." He
spat neatly to one side. "Privateer, if I ever saw one."


"How can you tell?" I asked.


He did not take his eyes from the horizon.


"She's built for running down fat merchantmen," he said. "Long in the bow,
flyers aloft, and she keeps her weather like a cat. Mark my words, she saw
a lone sloop and thought herself lucky."


Barnes, further down the line, let out a quiet snort.


"Poor las," he said. "She's not had the pleasure of our company yet."


The jest earned a few short, tight smiles. No one laughed out loud.


Orders rose into the air again. The guns were run out proper now, their
black mouths peering from the ports like watching eyes. Powder monkeys were
sent below to the magazine.


The air thickened with the smell of slow matches and oiled iron. Forward,
the capstan crew waited in readiness, knowing any sudden command might have
them heaving at the bars to shift the ship's weight by brute force alone.


Hale's voice came once more.


"Mr Merrick, bring her round a point. I'll not offer them a full broadside
until I see their colours."


"Aye, sir."


The deck heeled under us as the helm answered, and the brig's aspect
shifted in the gunport frame. She had come closer while we made ready. I
could see the line of her stem now, the lean sharpness of her, and the
glimmer of movement on her crowded deck.


My stomach made a slow, unhelpful roll.


"Eyes on your work, Kit," the gun captain said under his breath.


"Aye," I said again, and this time it felt like a promise to myself as much
as to him.


The brig showed no sign of changing course. If anything, she seemed to
swell as she came on, her sails drawing hard, her bow wave throwing spray
aside in little torn wings.


"Still no colours," someone said from aft.


"Then she'll have none to show," another replied darkly.


The next moments seemed to stretch out thin, like gold beat so fine you can
see light through it. The sound of the sea against the hull was loud, and
my own pulse felt as if it had moved into my throat.


Then, suddenly, it seemed like everything happened at once.


The brig fired first. We saw the flash along her side like a line of summer
lightning, then the roar followed, only half a breath later. Shot hummed
overhead; one ball struck the water short, throwing up a fountain of spray
that rattled against our flank. Another whined off the quarter, close
enough that I flinched in spite of myself.


"Steady!" came the shout. "Hold your fire till the word!"


Kingfisher shuddered, but she held her course.


Out of the corner of my eye I saw Broome's men bring their muskets up,
their faces pale but set. Maddox's jaw was clenched, but his hands moved
with their usual ease as he checked a lock here, a strap there.


"Run out!" the gun captain barked. Our crew heaved; the carriage thumped
forward, trucks biting the deck. The gun's muzzle slid out until it looked
cleanly down its chosen patch of sea, waiting.


Somewhere behind us, Captain Hale's voice cut through the hammering of my
blood.


"Very well," he said, in that calm, schoolroom tone of his. "Mr. Merrick,
give her our compliments."


"Fire!" Merrick roared.


Our first broadside went out with a noise that felt as if it had torn the
world in half. The gun leapt back against its tackle; smoke burst thick and
blinding through the port, rolling back in on us. The air stank of powder,
hot and bitter. My ears rang. For a moment I could not see the brig at all,
only a bright smear where the sea had been.


"Reload! Smartly now!"


My hands moved on their own: worm, sponge, charge, wad, shot, ram. The
drill we had done a hundred times now slapped into place over my fear like
a well-fitted lid. I knew the feel of the sponge pole, the weight of the
charge bag, the right sound the rammer made when the powder was properly
home. Men grunted, cursed hoarsely when the recoil caught them unprepared,
but they kept at it.


Through the clearing smoke I saw the brig again. We had marked her. A
splintered rail, a torn scrap where one of her stays had parted. Men were
running along her deck in disarray. A far cry from the discipline of our
crew.


But it was not enough. She turned her head a fraction and kept coming.


"Again!" came the cry.


We set our shoulders and obeyed.


* * *


The second broadside hit them harder.


I could feel it in the way their brig shuddered across the water: a tremor
that ran through her spars and into the wind itself. But she was still
coming on, angling toward our bow now, like a wolf that had taken a blow
and decided the only answer was to bite deeper.


The smoke clung to us. It drifted low along the deck, stirred by running
boots and the sea breeze but never quite leaving. My throat burned with
each breath, and every blink stung. Someone near me coughed hard enough to
double over, but there was no stopping. The next round had to be ready, and
the next after that.


"Keep your powder dry!" the gun captain shouted. "Mind the air! Don't let a
spark lie!"


Croft's voice came next, sharper, from somewhere to my left.


"Kit! Head down!"


I ducked instinctively. Something hissed past my ear; splinters, or shot, I
couldn't tell. The sound it made was small, but it felt close enough to
graze the breath out of me.


When I glanced his way, Croft was hauling a coil of fallen line out of the
gangway so no one would trip on it. Sweat ran in pale trails through the
powder on his face. His eyes found mine only long enough to confirm I was
standing, then he turned back to the next thing that needed doing.


I'd had no idea how men fought like this before I saw it myself. Everything
happened too swiftly to be marked, yet each moment pressed itself deep
enough to leave a second life behind it.


The brig flashed again.


The thunder came a heartbeat later, and Kingfisher lurched. Something
cracked forward, loud, sharp, and final. Men shouted. A block fell from
aloft and hit the deck with a force that shook my boots.


"Down! Down!" someone bellowed.


But it was too late for the man beneath the fallen rope. I saw only his
legs first, bent wrong. Then the rest of him came into view as men rushed
to haul the heavy tackle aside. His shirt was darkening too quickly for any
surgeon's skill to reach him.


I swallowed hard, tasting copper at the back of my tongue.


"Mind your eyes, Kit," the gun captain said, but softer this time. "Do your
work. That's how you live."


So I turned back to the gun.


The world narrowed to the chores of hands and the orders shouted in my ear;
pass the charge, ram it home, step aside, stand clear. The sea heaved under
us, knocking us one way then the other. My shoulder slammed against the
bulkhead once hard enough to leave a bruise. A lad beside me slipped on a
smear of water; I caught his arm before he slid into the gun's recoil path.


"Thank ye!" he gasped.


"Aye," I managed.


The brig was nearly upon us now, her bow shouldering the waves aside with
ugly determination. I could see faces on her deck; bearded men with
bandanas round their heads, jackets open, muskets raised. Some
shouted. Others smiled.


Privateers. No doubt of it now, their bayonets fixed, muskets levelled, the
boarding line ready at their rail. My pulse hammered in my throat.


"Marines!" Mr Merrick's voice rang sharp as cut steel. "Make ready!"


Broome and his men stepped to the gangway. Their bayonets caught the light
in a bright row. Maddox shouted something low to them; the line tightened,
steadied, braced.


The brig veered in, and for a breath, everything went almost quiet.


I could hear the sea more than the guns; the slap of waves against the
hull, the rattle of loose canvas aloft, the creak of the yards. Even the
wind seemed to be holding its breath.


The gun captain leaned close, voice rough.


"When she's near enough to smell her, lad... brace yourself. It'll come all
at once."


And it did.


The privateer fired a last raking shot at our bow, and we answered with a
broadside so thunderous it felt as if the deck leapt under my feet. Our gun
slammed back, tackle screaming. Smoke engulfed us, a choking wall that
burned the eyes. Men shouted through it. Somewhere, a man screamed.


Then shapes burst through the smoke, dark, fast, and leaping.


"Repel boarders!" Merrick roared.


They came over the rail with hooked pikes and blades, some wild-eyed,
others grim, all of them certain their daring would take us.


The first landed almost atop me. He swung a cutlass so suddenly that I felt
the wind of it pass my cheek. I scrambled back, tripping over the gun's
carriage. He followed fast, his teeth bared.


Croft hit him before he reached me. It happened too quickly to follow. One
moment the privateer was lunging; the next Croft was there, driving his
shoulder into the man's chest and sending them both hard onto the
deck. They grappled, boots slipping on the wet planks.


The man snarled and thrust upward with his blade; Croft twisted, pinned the
arm with his knee, and struck him square across the jaw with the heel of
his hand.


The man went limp.


"Up," Croft barked at me, dragging me by the arm with a force that snapped
me out of my shock. "Keep behind the gun!"


"Aye!" I gasped.


The deck was chaos. Muskets fired at point-blank, and bayonets locked with
blades. The marines held the gangway like a wall. Broome fought with a
stillness that was terrible to watch; each thrust precise, each step
measured. Maddox moved quicker, more forcefully, knocking a boarder back
with a crack of his musket's butt before firing the next at arm's length.


The brig's own guns were too close to bear now. The battle had become a
tangle of bodies and breath.


A man screamed near the forecastle. Another fell beside the pump, blood
pouring from his shoulder. A shot hit the shrouds above, showering us with
splinters. The air was filled with the salt-metal stink of blood and
powder.


"Kit!" someone yelled.


I turned just in time to see a privateer level a pistol in one hand, a
boarding axe in his other. The shot cracked beside my ear, splintering the
rail in an explosion of sharp wood. Something struck my cheek, hot,
blinding, and I staggered, hand to my face as blood ran fresh between my
fingers.


The man drew back to swing his axe, but before he could manage it, Barnes
slammed into him from the side, roaring something wordless. The two crashed
into the bulwark, the axe and gun clattering away. Barnes struck him twice,
quick and hard, and the man dropped.


Barnes looked back at me, panting.


"You all right, lad?"


"Aye. A scratch."


He gave a sharp nod. "Good. Keep your feet."


I touched my cheek. My fingers came away red.


Everything felt louder suddenly, the crash of bodies, the ring of steel,
the shouted orders. But under it all, Kingfisher held fast. Her crew fought
like a single creature with a hundred limbs.


And slowly, the brig's men faltered.


First one, then two broke back toward their rail. One leapt for the rope
they had thrown across. Another stumbled as Broome's bayonet took him in
the arm. Maddox fired once more, smoke blooming round him.


"Drive them off!" Merrick shouted. "To the rail with them!"


Kingfisher's men surged forward. The privateers hesitated, just long
enough.


A swell struck the brig's side, pitching her away. The gap widened by
inches, then feet. A man jumped for the rope and missed, splashing into the
sea between us. Others scrambled back, shouting for their own helmsman.


Then, suddenly, the brig's bow veered off.


She turned hard, as if her courage had snapped in half.


"Let her go!" Hale called. "Guns hold your fire! Marines, stand down!"


The boarders still on our deck were quickly subdued, disarmed, or dragged
under guard. One tried to swing again and was knocked breathless by
Maddox's fist. Another was pinned by two hands at once.


Within moments, the fight was over.


The brig cast away, limping on splintered timbers, her sails torn, her crew
shouting in disarray. She had not expected a King's sloop to bite back so
fiercely. She fled rather than try her luck again.


The silence afterward felt immense. My ears still rang. My hands
shook. Smoke curled in soft ribbons above the deck, and the smell of blood
hung thick as fog. Men stood where they'd fought, chests heaving, weapons
slick with sweat.


Croft came to me first.


His eyes swept my face, taking in the cut at once. He reached out, thumb
brushing just beside the wound, then tilted my chin to see better.


"You're hurt," he said, voice low.


"It's nothing," I managed.


"It's not nothing." His jaw tightened. "Come on. We're taking you to the
surgeon before it starts swelling."


"I can walk."


"I know," he said, quiet enough that only I could hear. "You'll walk with
me."


I followed him toward the quarterdeck companionway, where other wounded
were already being taken below.


Behind us, the crew set to clearing the dead and tending to the living. The
brig dwindled in the distance, limping like a beaten hound.


Kingfisher had held. And I had felt the ship's teeth in me too. Not badly,
all in all. Certainly not grave, but enough to mark me well enough.


* * *


The steps down to the orlop felt narrower than I remembered, as if the
battle had shaken the ship until every passage had shrunk. My legs
trembled, though I hoped no one could see it in the dim light. The smell
changed as we descended: smoke fading, pitch and camphor thickening, and
under it all that clean sharp edge of spirits and linen.


Croft kept close to me. Not touching, but near enough that I could feel the
warmth off him. Another wounded man stumbled past us on the ladder.


The surgeon's mate looked up as we arrived, his sleeves already rolled, his
hands wet from the last man he'd seen. His gaze flicked to my cheek and
sharpened.


"Get him on the chest," he said.


"I'm not bad," I insisted.


"I'll be the judge of that," he replied, not unkindly.


Croft steered me toward a low bench beside a lantern. The place was
cramped, a tangle of hammocks folded up out of the way, chests pushed back,
crates stacked high with bandages and jars. The surgeon himself was bent
over a marine with a piece of splintered wood lodged in his shoulder. Blood
soaked the man's coat. Broome stood nearby, steadying him with that calm he
had, murmuring something I couldn't hear.


"Sit," Croft said.


I sat.


He crouched in front of me so the surgeon's mate would have a better angle
at my face. From this close, the lamplight caught the powder streaks on his
jaw, the tightness at the corner of his mouth.


"You needn't hover," I said softly.


His eyes lifted to mine. "I'll hover if I please."


The surgeon's mate cleaned the cut quickly, briskly, with a cloth soaked in
spirits. The burn of it made me hiss through my teeth. Croft didn't look
away from the wound, as if watching it made certain nothing worse would
happen.


"Lucky," the mate said. "A bit deeper and you'd be dripping like that poor
bastard over there."


He nodded toward the marine being stitched.


"Will it scar?" I asked.


"Most things at sea do," the mate said simply. "Hold still."


He applied a strip of lint and tied it off with twine behind my ear. The
pressure was oddly comforting.


Corbyn finished with the marine and crossed to us, wiping his hands.


"What's this one?" he asked.


"Splintered wood from a pistol shot, sir." the mate said. "Just a scrape."


Corbyn examined my cheek with a practised eye. "You'll keep the side of
your face, at any rate." His tone was dry, but his touch was
gentle. "You'll live, Kittridge. Go easy for a day. If the swelling
worsens, come back. The spirits should keep it from suppurating."


"Aye, sir."


Corbyn moved on to the next man.


Croft rose, grinning. "Your first wound in battle, lad. Come on," he
said. "Air's better above."


We made our way back toward the ladder. The ship creaked around us, the
kind of sound I recognised now; Kingfisher settling herself after her
strain. Above, the smell of gunpowder still drifted on the air, but there
was less shouting, fewer hurried boots. Men were beginning to speak again,
low and tired and alive.


On deck, the wind hit my face cleanly. The cut throbbed with the change in
the air, but it felt good in a way.


The sea was dark, but calmer than before. The privateer was a distant smear
on the horizon now. Lanterns swung gently along the rail, casting soft gold
arcs across the wreckage of the fight. Loose rope, splintered wood, dropped
weapons, signs of fury fading into the aftermath.


Barnes crossed the waist, wiping his brow with the back of his wrist. When
he saw me, he slowed.


"How's the face, lad?" he asked.


"Still attached," I said.


He grinned. "Good man."


"Feels different now," I said quietly. "The ship."


"Aye. First fight'll do that."


"Is it always like this?"


Barnes breathed in through his nose, considering.


"No. Sometimes it's worse," he said. "Sometimes easier. But the
first... you'll remember every beat of it, whether you want to or not."


I looked down at my hands. "I thought... during it... I thought I might
disgrace myself. Freeze."


"But you didn't." His voice was low and certain. "You kept your head. You
kept working. That's the making of a sailor."


Croft stepped a little closer, the two of them quite close now.


"Kit... listen to me, lad."


I raised my eyes.


"You're allowed to feel it after," Croft said. "We all do. It'll stay with
you for a few days. Maybe more. Just your head catching up with your body."


The breeze caught a loose coil of his hair where it had come free of its
tie. I wanted to reach for it, but I didn't. Not here.


"I'm glad you were near," I said instead.


"So am I," he answered.


A moment passed between us that felt like a held breath. Then the boatswain
called orders forward, and the spell eased.


"Up, lad," Croft said. "Let's see if Merrick wants the lines reset."


I nodded and moved with him toward the forecastle. The ache in my cheek
pulsed with each step, but the fear had left me now. Only the memory
remained, smoke, steel, the shock of the blow, Croft's hands on me.


By the time the sun dipped and the lamps were lit for the night, the deck
was washed, the dead taken below, and a hush lay over the crew; a hush born
of exhaustion, gratitude, and the kind of nearness men only feel when
they've glimpsed the abyss and stepped back from it.


Croft was gentle with me that night. He drew me into his hammock, molding
himself against me, our chests together, thighs crossed over each other,
feet touching, and my head nestled into the dark fur of his chest.


He was careful not to bump my cheek, even when our lips met. There was a
quiet desperation in him now, a passion borne of an imagined future that
Croft had escaped; a future in which the boarder's pistol had struck true
and erased me from the crew roster of Kingfisher.


I fell asleep with his lips pressed to the blond curls at the top of my
head, and felt safe for the first time since the battle's end.


I remember sensing that at long last, I belonged to Kingfisher and her
crew.


And to him.


* * *
