The last line came off the bollard with a wet slap, and Kingfisher drifted
clear of the quay like a man drawing breath. The harbour lay around us in a
ring of lanterns, the shore lights blurred in the gathering dark, the
silhouettes of other ships standing out against the sky. You could feel the
air change once we let go of the land. It carried more salt and less smoke,
and there was a sense of space beneath it, as if the night itself grew
taller over the water.


"Hands to the capstan! Heave there... walk her round!"


The order went up from Merrick, the master of the ship, and the men moved
without hurry or fuss. Boots thudded, and the capstan bars clacked into
their sockets. Shoulders leaned together and the cable creaked in the
hawse. I took a bar with three others, both hands closing around worn
wood. The old chant began low under our breath, more rhythm than song, and
the capstan turned slowly as the ship's head swung.


Across from me, Croft walked the circle with that balanced, confident
stride of his, the muscles of his forearms flexing under bare skin. He had
his sleeves rolled high, and the lantern hanging from the deckhead picked
out the dark hair on his arms and the gleam of sweat along them. His shirt
gaped a little at the neck from the work, showing the thick fur of his
chest and the strong line where it narrowed down.


He looked good. He always did when he was working hard. It did something to
me when these moments passed.


He caught me looking as he came round again, one corner of his mouth
tugging upward, but he didn't say anything. He only shifted his grip on the
bar, shoulders tightening, and the line of his body under the thin linen
took on that taut, deliberate shape I knew from very different
circumstances.


I put my eyes on the deck and leaned into the push.


We freed the anchor, braced the yards, and turned her head for the harbour
mouth. Sails cracked and filled above us. Lines hissed through blocks, and
the ship heeled a little as the first clean breath of wind caught her
broadside. The shore slid away, quay and warehouse and tavern light falling
behind into a smear of yellow and black.


"Mind the slack there, Kit!" Barnes shouted, pointing with his chin.


"Got it," I called back, already on the move.


I stooped for the line he'd meant and took up the coil, lifting it to my
shoulder. As I straightened, a shadow fell across me; Croft stepped in
close to take the other end. "You heard the man," he said. "We'll have it
tidy."


"Aye," I managed.


We went to the rail together to make off the line. The deck gave a gentle
swing beneath us as Kingfisher answered the sea. I moved on instinct now,
the weight changing under my boots.


Croft stood to my left, shoulders almost touching mine, the heat of him a
steady presence at my side. His hands worked quickly and sure, wrapping the
rope into a neat figure-eight. I could smell salt and tar and the faint
human warmth of him under it all.


"You're watching your feet more than you used to," he murmured.


"Is that a complaint?"


"A compliment," he said. "You didn't fall on your arse once."


"The night is young," I muttered.


He laughed under his breath. The sound was soft and rough and stupidly dear
to me. He finished the last turn of the coil and hung it on the pin, then
straightened, stretching his arms above his head so his shirt rode up over
his stomach.


He held the stretch just long enough to give me an eyeful.


The lantern-light caught the lines of him; the long muscles of his abdomen,
the hair across it narrowing down into the waistband of his slops. I could
see where the fabric clung to his hip-bones, where the fur thinned along
the edges. A flash of skin I knew the taste of now, and wanted again with
an ache that felt older than the month and a half I'd been aboard.


He pretended to look up at the rigging, as though the stretch was for his
back alone.


"Wind's veering a touch," he said.


"Aye," I answered, and my voice came out hoarser than it ought to. "I see
it."


He dropped his arms and glanced at me sidelong, eyes quick and sharp as a
knife. He'd heard my voice. He'd felt the way I'd gone still next to him
when he bared himself. He knew what he'd done, the scoundrel.


The slow, satisfied curve that found his mouth then was answer enough.


Two can play that game, I thought.


Barnes had us to the braces again, shifting the yards as we neared the
harbour mouth. I took my place on the line beside Croft. The rope pulled
tight; we set our feet and leaned, shoulder against shoulder this time,
bodies braced together against the strain. The contact ran from my upper
arm down through my chest and into my belly in one unbroken line. He
pressed just enough to make sure I knew he was there. Not enough to draw
comment from anyone else.


"Brace there!" Barnes called. "Belay!"


We eased off, hands working fast to make the line secure. My fingers
tingled.


Ahead, the breakwater walls loomed up on either bow: black stone with a
pale line of foam breaking at their feet, two lanterns burning at their
ends like watchful eyes. Beyond that lay nothing but open water, dark and
restless under a nighttime sky.


The ship passed between them with a slight lift, like a man stepping from
one room into another.


The swell changed once we cleared the last of the harbour. There was more
weight to it, more roll; Kingfisher dipped her bow and rose again, her
timbers giving a low, pleased groan. The wind came cleaner, carrying cold
in it from somewhere far out.


"Now you'll see her proper," Croft said, voice low and full of a pride that
had nothing to do with rank. "Harbour's for tying up. This is where she
lives."


"You sound offended we kept her at the quay at all," I said.


He shrugged one shoulder. "She bears it. But she likes this better."


He moved a half-step closer as the deck tilted. It was natural on the face
of it, two men adjusting to the same movement, but he let his hip find mine
and stay there a moment, the pressure light but insistent.


I let my weight lean back into him, just enough for him to feel that I'd
chosen it.


"Don't you?" he asked.


"I do," I said.


His mouth turned up again, faint and wicked.


Merrick's voice cut across the deck: "That'll do, lads. Keep a good eye on
your lashings. We're for sea now, so don't leave aught loose that shouldn't
swing."


There was the usual murmur of "Aye, aye, sir," and the men began to break
away in pairs and threes, some heading to their posts for the next watch,
some to the hatchway and the comfort of their hammocks. The work eased, the
noise with it. Above us, the rigging sang in a different tone now, a
higher, thinner music as the wind took full hold.


Barnes came past and flicked my ear with two fingers.


"You've got your sea-legs at last," he said. "I shan't have to tie you on
to the rail."


"I'd thank you not to," I answered.


"We'll see," he said, amused, and moved on.


Croft watched him go, then leaned in. "You heard the man," he
murmured. "You don't fall over now. I've plans for you that don't involve
you going head-first into a scupper."


"You're very free with your plans," I said softly.


"Only with you," he replied.


My heart jumped.


He could not have chosen a worse thing to say if he meant me to keep my
wits. Or a better one, if he meant to see what he could draw out of me.


I took a breath and let my gaze drop deliberately, running from his face
down his throat to the open collar of his shirt, taking in the way the
lantern light caught on the twist of hair there. I let my eyes linger, just
long enough to make it plain that I knew what he was doing, that I
remembered his body from the dark of the sail locker and under the
hammock's canvas curve.


When I looked back up, his eyes had gone darker.


"Come on," he said after a moment, straightening. "We've done our part. Let
the watch-keepers earn their grog. We've a bit of night left yet to lie
down and think on respectable things."


"I've never known you to think on anything respectable," I said.


"Oh I do, lad," he answered. "But I don't tell you about those."


He stepped toward the hatch. I followed, the roll of the ship and the
warmth of his nearness guiding my feet as much as the ladder rungs.


As we reached the coaming, the ship took a deeper swell and I shifted
aside, making a point of letting my hip move in front of him. The slops
slid along my skin, fabric drawing in across the curve of my arse and the
cleft for a moment before loosening again.


I felt his gaze land there like a touch.


I did not look back. I only stepped down into the dark, pulse hammering,
knowing full well I'd just given him something to think on that had nothing
to do with respectability.


He came after me, close enough that his hand found the centre of my back as
we descended the ladder.


"Mind yourself, Kit," he said softly.


"I am," I answered, and this time I let him hear the double meaning plain.


His fingers tightened for a heartbeat, then loosened again.


We went down together into the dim swing of the hammock tier, and the ship
around us leaned her shoulder into the open sea.


* * *


By the time we arrived below, the lower deck was halfway to sleep.


Lanterns hung at intervals along the beams, their light thrown upward so
the hammocks glowed pale in rows, each one a soft, moving shape barely
hiding the body inside.


The air was thick with canvas and sweat and the faint tang of vinegar used
on the planks earlier in the day. Men talked in low voices, some sitting on
sea-chests in their shirtsleeves, some already flat on their backs in the
sway of their slings, hands folded over their stomachs.


The bell sounded from above, two short strokes, and the sound came down
through the timbers like a reminder that time still moved in its own
fashion, no matter what we did with it.


"Make room there," someone called as we ducked into our tier.


"You're late to your bed, Croft," another voice said. "Thought you'd
shipped yourself off to the gunroom."


"Wish I had," Croft replied easily. "They've chairs there. You lot don't
own so much as a stool between you."


There was a ripple of laughter.


Barnes sat on a chest near the main hatch, boots off, sleeves rolled,
washing his feet in a small tub with an expression of deep, quiet
pleasure. He lifted his chin at us as we passed.


"About time," he said. "You'll be no use in the morning if you lie awake
admirin' the moon."


"Haven't seen the moon," I said.


"You've seen enough else," he answered, and I couldn't tell if the look he
shot between me and Croft meant anything more than a general dig.


If it did, he kept it to himself.


Croft huffed and moved on, ducking under a hammock as he went. I followed,
feeling more observed than I had any right to feel in that dim light.


Our own slings hung near the middle of the tier, side by side in the long
row, canvas already swaying a little with the ship's roll. The sound of
snoring came from the far end, mixed with the soft creak of rope and
intermittent sighs as men turned in their sleep.


Croft pulled his shirt over his head in one smooth movement, muscles in his
back bunching with the motion. The lantern-light picked out the shape of
him: shoulders broad and powerful. Scars traced pale lines through the hair
in places, an old rope-burn, a healed cut, marks of work and weather.


He shook the shirt out once and threw it negligently over the line strung
beneath his hammock. Then he reached for the waistband of his slops and
untied the cord, letting the fabric fall a little to sit low on his hips as
he loosened it, not dropping them fully yet. He was in no hurry. Not now.


"You look worn," he said, as if he weren't the most distracting thing in
the room.


"So do you," I managed.


He snorted. "I always do."


"That's not what I meant," I said before I had time to think better of it.


He stilled. Looked over his shoulder at me, dark hair falling loose around
his face. His eyes found mine and held them for a long moment. There was
something in that look that went far beyond teasing, though the teasing was
there too, simmering just beneath.


"Isn't it," he said softly.


I felt the heat rise up my throat.


Around us, one of the men cursed about misplacing his shirt; someone
laughed and tossed it to him out of the dark. Sutter's voice drifted over
from three hammocks down, telling a low story about a tavern girl who'd
turned out to be a tavern boy after all. It drew another round of quiet
laughter. No one was listening to us. Not properly.


I pulled my own shirt off, working it over my head and shaking out my
hair. The air was cool against my skin, a relief after the heat of the day,
but the relief didn't last long. Not with Croft standing half-dressed three
feet away, waist bare and slops hanging just far enough down to show the
deep V-narrowing of his hips.


He knew exactly what he was doing.


I bent to unfasten my own waistband, fingers working the knot loose. As I
did, I made a small adjustment -- subtle, but deliberate. The fabric at the
back of my slops drew tight across the curve of me as I bent, the cloth
dipping into the cleft just enough to show its shape, and part of the
smooth twin globes revealing themselves above the sagging material. The
thin material left very little to the imagination for anyone at the right
angle.


Which Croft was.


I could hear it in his breath. A very faint sound, but I knew it now, the
way I knew the sound of rope through a block or the first whisper of a gust
in the rigging.


I straightened slowly, letting the slops fall back into place, then turned
as if nothing had happened. I stretched slowly, languorously, letting the
muscles in my arms and chest move under the skin for him. His eyes drank me
in like warm whiskey.


"What?" I asked, as innocently as I could manage.


He was watching me with an intensity that made my skin prickle. His jaw had
tightened a little, a cord of muscle standing out near his ear.


"Nothing," he said at last, voice lower. "You're... getting the hang of the
ship."


"Thought you said I'd already got it," I replied.


"You're improving," he said. The words themselves were mild; the way he
said them was not.


I stepped nearer under the pretence of reaching for the line where I'd hang
my shirt. As I did, my bare shoulder made contact with his chest. I felt
the hair there rasp against my skin, the sensation lewd and delicious.


"You did that on purpose," he murmured, too soft for anyone else to hear.


"I don't know what you mean," I said.


He laughed, a raw, quiet sound that never reached his mouth. His hand came
up and caught my wrist for the briefest moment, thumb pressing into the
inside of it where the skin was thin. The contact went straight to my
pulse.


"If you keep on," he said, "I'll forget we're not alone."


"You won't," I whispered.


"Won't I?"


The way he said it left very little doubt that he could be persuaded.


Across the tier, Barnes cracked some joke about a man's snoring being
louder than a gun. The laugh that followed it snapped the moment in two;
Croft dropped my wrist and sat back on his chest, lifting one leg into his
hammock to give himself an excuse to look away.


I swallowed, heart pounding, and forced myself to move as well.


I folded my shirt and tucked it behind the head of my sling, then climbed
into the canvas and lay back. The hammock took my weight with that familiar
soft lurch, the planks wavering at the edges of my vision as the ship
rolled. I hooked one ankle over the other to steady myself and let my arm
fall across my ribs.


Croft swung up into his own with easy grace, the canvas dipping and rising
under him. For a moment he lay still, one forearm under his head, a dark
patch of hair shadowing his arm pit, tempting between the risk of his chest
muscle and the swell of his tricep. The other hand resting low on his
stomach where the hair thickened again beneath the line of his
slops. Lantern-light skated across the muscles there. He made no effort to
hide himself; he never did, when he knew I was watching.


"Comfortable?" he asked.


"No," I said, too quickly.


He smiled sideways at me. "Liar."


I hesitated, then shrugged with the shoulder nearest him, the movement
making the canvas sway.


"It's warm," I said. "And loud. And there's a man three slings down who
sounds like he's choking to death every time he snores."


"That's Higgs," Croft said. "He's been choking to death for ten
years. Don't worry about him."


"I'm not," I whispered. "I'm worried about waking up tangled round you with
Barnes watching."


He went very still at that. Then he turned his head on the canvas and
looked at me properly.


"You say that as though it'd trouble you."


"It might trouble you," I said.


He considered me for a long breath.


"I've had worse troubles," he said quietly. "And I woke up smoking after
those."


My lips twitched in spite of myself.


"You don't smoke."


"Not tobacco," he said, and there it was again; that quick, wicked glint
that reminded me exactly how last night had gone and what we had done with
the safety of dark canvas around us.


Heat licked low in my belly.


I shifted, letting my hips move just enough that the hammock swung, the
fabric of my slops drawing along me again. It wasn't much, but it was
enough to make his gaze flick down, then back up.


"We could test it," I said before I lost my nerve. "Whether you mind or
not. Waking up tangled."


He stared at me, then huffed out a breath that might have been a laugh and
might have been something closer to surrender.


"Not tonight," he said. "You'll want a clear head for the morrow. Merrick's
twice as sharp the first day out, and Barnes follows his temper like a dog
to a bone."


I knew he was right. I didn't have to like it.


"I see," I said.


"But if you mean to go on teasing a man in his own tier," he added, "you'd
best expect him to pay you out for it when we've somewhere more private
than this."


"That sounds like a threat," I said.


He closed his eyes then, or seemed to. His breathing slowed, evening out in
that way sailors had of snatching sleep where they could. It was possible
he was only resting. It was possible he was wide awake, listening to every
shift I made on the sling. I couldn't tell.


I lay there in the swaying half-darkness, surrounded by the soft sounds of
men settling into sleep. My body buzzed with thwarted want and the memory
of Croft's hand on my wrist.


He'd started this game. I meant to learn to play it.


But for the moment, all I had was the creak of ropes, the sway of canvas,
and the knowledge that just an arm's length away, Thomas Croft lay in the
dark with his slops slung low on his hips, thinking God knew what about me.


It was enough to keep any man from sleep.


* * *


I hadn't planned to wander far from the berthing deck. The ship had that
soft, restless motion she always found after lights-out, when the day's
heat thinned and the boards creaked with cooler air settling through the
timbers. I only wanted a few minutes to settle myself; Croft had left me
wound tight in ways that no hammock could soothe.


I padded quietly along the lower deck, my shirt hanging open, the lantern
aft casting long, patient shadows. A handful of men slept in their
hammocks, barely more than shapes in dimness, the sway of canvas like
breathing. Someone snored gently. Someone else murmured a name I didn't
know. The whole of it felt suspended, like the ship was holding her breath
between tides.


I meant to turn back. Truly, I did. But then I heard it.


A faint, uneven thud from up ahead, softened by canvas, but strong enough
to carry through the stillness. I stopped without thinking. My pulse
quickened in a way I told myself was simple curiosity.


Another sound followed, lower, rougher. A growl. Something caught between
two throats, one human, one... not?


I stopped in front of the sail locker door. No lantern glowed beneath the
door's bottom. Only darkness, thick and unmoving. The noise came again,
clearer this time. A breath, short, hard, swallowed down as though someone
didn't wish it heard. Then a slow, dragging sound of weight shifting over
piled canvas.


I should have walked away. Not loudly, not hurried; just left. But I
didn't. Something pinned me there like a nail in a beam.


Then I heard the voice.


"Ahh, God..." Soft. Low. Ragged, and unmistakably Corporal Broome's voice.


The breath drained from my chest. It wasn't full, but the timbre... the
shape of it was his, deep enough to vibrate off the doorframe. And just as
I registered that, the low growl came again, closer this
time. Deep. Rolling. The sort of sound a dog makes when he's keyed up and
doesn't know what to do with himself.


My jaw dropped. It couldn't be.


My stomach lurched, because the two sounds didn't belong together; not like
that, not in a dark room behind a half-latched door, not with the boards
giving the faintest suggestion of rhythmic pressure.


"Christ..." I whispered before I could stop myself.


Inside, another muffled thump. A low, wordless noise that might've been
pleasure. Or strain. Or someone trying not to cry out.


My heart pounded loud enough to drown out everything else. I lifted a hand,
God knows why, as if touching the door might answer any question that my
senses couldn't.


Something brushed my knee.


I flinched so hard I hit my shoulder on a hanging spar, biting back a yelp.


Joss sat neatly at my side, tail thumping once against the boards.


My brain emptied clean. He blinked up at me, tongue just visible, eyes soft
and expectant. A moment later, he gave a soft huff, the kind that said
`Well? What the bloody hell are we doing?'


If Joss was here, warm, real, tongue lolling, then whatever was growling
inside the sail locker was... not him.


The noises inside didn't stop. If anything, they sharpened; a low, rhythmic
grunt, a thud like someone's back meeting the bulkhead, an unmistakable
catch of breath swallowed too late.


Joss nudged my hand, tail sweeping again.


"Oh God," I breathed.


He trotted off a few paces, paused, then looked back as if inviting me to
follow, as if this were any normal evening walk.


I stared at the sail locker door a moment longer, heat climbing my neck
like fire under my collar. There was no mistaking any of it now. Broome was
in there with someone. Someone big enough to make the boards shake. Someone
who...


I swallowed hard.


I backed away slowly, step by careful step. Joss padded after me, falling
into heel with the confidence of a creature who knew every inch of the ship
better than I did.


At the ladder, I let out a breath I didn't know I'd been holding. My palms
were damp. My shirt clung to my spine. My legs felt strangely hollow.


I climbed back up to the berthing deck, trying to compose my face, too
late.


Croft was already waiting.


He leaned against the frame of the bulkhead, arms folded, ankles crossed,
shirt open enough at the throat to show that dark line of hair tracking
down his chest. His eyes found mine the second I stepped onto the
deck. Even in the dim glow from the single lantern, I saw the shift; the
slight narrowing, the intent focus.


"Kit," he murmured. "What's happened?"


I froze.m He pushed off the bulkhead and took two slow steps toward me,
studying every inch of my expression the way a man might study weather on
the horizon.


"You're white as canvas," he said. "And red as a lantern. Both at
once. That's quite a feat."


"I... No. It's nothing. I only..."


"Not nothing." He lifted a hand, brushed a lock of hair from my
forehead. "Tell me."


My pulse thudded against his touch.


"I don't even know how to explain it," I muttered.


"Try."


"I heard something."


"Where, lad?"


"Sail locker."


That stopped him.


His brow lifted, slow and deliberate. "Oh?"


"Yes."


"What sort of something?"


My face burned hotter. "Croft... don't make me say it."


"Oh, I'll absolutely make you say it."


I groaned and covered my face. "You're impossible."


"And you're stalling." He slipped two fingers under my chin and tilted my
face up. "Tell me what you heard."


"I heard Broome."


A beat of silence, then, "Corporal Broome. In the sail locker?"


"Yes."


"Alone?"


"No."


Croft's mouth twitched. "Go on."


"There was a... a sound."


"What sort of sound?"


I shut my eyes. "A growl. I heard something growling."


He blinked once.


Then twice.


And then his lips parted in the slowest, most delighted grin I'd ever seen
crawl across his face.


"No," he whispered. "You didn't."


"I did," I hissed. "And then Joss walked up behind me and I nearly died on
the spot."


Croft pressed a fist to his mouth, shoulders shaking with suppressed
laughter. "Oh, poor Kit. Poor, sweet, naïve Kit. You thought--"


"Don't say it," I warned, mortified.


"You thought Broome was..."


"Croft..."


"rutting with his dog?"


I groaned so loudly I startled a man sleeping in a nearby hammock. I spun
and shoved my face into Croft's chest to hide the colour rising in me.


He wrapped his arms around my shoulders, laughter muffled against my hair.


"Kit, lad," he laughed, his breath warm against my ear. "What am I going to
do with you?"


"Throw me overboard," I muttered. "It's no less than I deserve."


"Hardly." His thumb stroked behind my ear in a way that made my knees
tremble. "That's the funniest thing I've heard in a month."


"It's not funny."


He kissed my temple. "It's very funny."


"I was genuinely mortified."


"Of what? That a marine had taken a liking to four-legged company?"


"Croft!"


He grinned like a devil. "Well? You stood outside listening."


"I didn't mean to listen!" I protested. "I thought something was wrong!"


"But something was very right," he said smoothly.


That shut me up.


He laughed again, low and wicked. "Come here. Let me tell you who Broome
was in there with."


My heart jumped. "You know?"


"Oh aye." His fingers trailed down the back of my neck, tugging lightly at
my hair. "And you won't believe it."


"Who?"


"Sergeant Maddox."


I stared.


He nodded, wickedly pleased. "Our sergeant likes to
make... noises. He's... a growler, you might say. Broome told me once. It's
one of the worst-kept secrets aboard."


"Good God."


"You heard half of it." He brushed his lips by my cheek, not quite
touching. "Be glad you didn't hear the rest."


I wanted to sink through the deck.


Croft chuckled softly and tugged me toward him, his lips brushing my
jaw. "Come on, lad. Follow me. Let's get you someplace where you can
compose yourself."


* * *


The ladder down into the bilge was steeper than I liked at the best of
times. Tonight it felt steeper still. The lantern Croft carried swung on
its hook from his hand, casting yellow arcs over the timbers and catching
in the wet sheen along some of the beams. The air grew heavier with each
rung; warmer, damper, steeped in tar and old water and that sour tang of
the ship's belly.


"Watch your step," he said over his shoulder. "Last thing I want is to have
to explain to Hale why one of his sailors snapped his neck in the guts of
the ship."


"I'll tell him it was your fault," I muttered. "You dragged me down here."


He gave a small grunt of amusement and stepped off the last rung into the
narrow passage. "You've spent all day dragging me about with your eyes,
teasing me, showing off. Seems only fair I repay the favour."


The bilge tunnel ran low and tight between the curving frames of the
hull. I had to duck my head in places; Croft's shoulders nearly brushed
both sides if he wasn't careful. Pools of stagnant water lay in shallow
dips, reflecting the lantern in broken smears. Rat droppings, stray oakum,
bits of old rope--it was not a place any man came for pleasure.


Which, naturally, was why we were here. Of course, for a ship of the line,
Kingfisher's bilge was clean as a whistle. Most mornings, at least two
sailors were down here scrubbing. Captain Hale didn't fancy filth on his
ship, and the sailors followed his lead religiously.


Croft led the way a little farther along until he found a kind of hollow
where two frames met and formed a half-sheltered pocket. Above us, through
the boards and beams, I could hear the muffled creak of the lower deck:
someone turning in a hammock, a faint thud of a bare foot hitting wood, the
ghost of a cough. All of it distant.


Croft hung the lantern on a nail driven into a rib, its light falling in a
soft circle over the two of us and the damp timbers.


He turned then and just looked at me.


It wasn't the easy appraisal he gave a new line or a bit of rigging. It was
the look I'd felt on me all damned day. Every time I'd bent to a coil of
rope, every time my shirt had ridden up, every time I'd leaned a little too
close behind him at the rail, that look had followed me. It had been a kind
of constant pressure between my shoulder blades. Now, down here, it faced
me full.


"You know exactly what you've been doing," he said quietly.


I wet my lips. "Do I?"


"Aye." He stepped closer, slow as a tide. "You do. You've had me half-hard
since breakfast, you menace."


I felt a treacherous, pleased warmth flush my face. "Seemed only fair. You
were the one laughing at me about Broome. And you did a bit of teasing
yourself, if you're honest."


He grinned, wicked and fond all at once. "You'll not let that go, will
you?"


"You told me I thought a man was..." I broke off, shaking my head. "No. I'm
not saying it again."


He chuckled under his breath and closed the last of the distance between
us. The beam at my back was slick in places where condensation had run
down; the wood was warm from the day's heat, though the air itself felt
close and cool against my skin.


Croft braced one hand beside my head, the other settling at my hip, thumb
rubbing just once along the waistband of my slops.


"Look at you," he murmured. "Still pink when I mention it."


"Wouldn't you be?"


"I wasn't the one who imagined Joss was in the locker."


"Croft."


He laughed softly, the sound low and pleased. "All right. I'll be kind. For
now."


"For now?"


He didn't answer with words. He answered by kissing me.


It was not a careful kiss, not the kind we might have traded in a dark
corner near the hammocks, quick and stolen before someone turned over. It
was full and claiming, his mouth moulding to mine like he'd had the shape
of it in his mind all day and was finally allowed to test it again. My
hands went to his shoulders automatically, fingers finding the familiar
lines there, the hard strength that came from hauling canvas and rope.


I opened to him without thinking, and he took the invitation as though it
were his due. The taste of him was salt and bread and the faint smoke of
the whiskey we'd had at supper. His stubble rasped my chin; his breath came
out in a quiet groan that I felt more than heard.


He pressed me back into the timber, his chest flat to mine, hips slotting
in close. The narrowness of the tunnel forced us together until there was
no space left at all. I felt his heart beating hard under my palms. Mine
matched it, a frantic echo.


"You've got no idea," he murmured against my mouth, "what you looked like
today. Sun on your shoulders. That damned shirt hanging off you. You cruel
boy."

footsteps. Hearing none, he climbed, then stepped aside to let me through.


The lower deck was dim but not asleep. A few lanterns burned low. Men
talked quietly at the mess table; someone sharpened a blade with long, even
strokes. A sailor nearby sat with his back to a cask, shirt undone halfway,
his mate leaning against him as they shared a tin of something warm.


No one looked at us twice.


Croft's eyes swept the deck, taking in who was awake, who was watching, who
wasn't. Then he turned to me, voice pitched low enough that only I could
hear.


"Come."


We crossed to the hammocks.


My own hung where it always did, a pale curve of canvas swaying lightly
with the ship. Croft's was two rows over.


He didn't go to his. He went to mine. Lifted the edge.


Then he looked back at me. "No fuss tonight," he said softly. "We've earned
our rest."


I climbed in first. He followed, slipping in behind me with an ease that
spoke of practice, though this was only the second time we'd done it. The
hammock dipped under our combined weight, the canvas wrapping around us
like a second skin.


His arm slid around my waist; his chest pressed lightly to my back. His
breath warmed the nape of my neck.


"Comfortable?" he murmured.


"Yes."


"Good."


He shifted once, settling himself, then rested his forehead against the
back of my shoulder. His hand splayed over my stomach, fingers drawing slow
circles that felt as intimate as anything in the locker; gentler, but no
less true.


The ship rocked. The canvas swayed. Croft's arm tightened slightly around
me. My eyes drifted closed.


Not long after, his breathing evened. I felt it against my back, slow and
certain, as though he trusted the world to keep its shape for a few hours.


Before I fell fast asleep, I had the strangest notion that he was inside
me. I could still feel the phantom of his long, thick cock, moving inside
me.


It was a feeling I didn't want to fade. Ever.


The night took us both.


* * *

Chapter 2


By the time the sun climbed toward the middle of the sky, Kingfisher had
taken on that slow, intent pace she wore between orders. The morning's
scrub was done, the pump left to drip, and most of the shout and rush had
eased into quieter work along the rails and at the guns.


The heat of the day pressed down through the rigging. Tar in the seams
softened and gave off its own sharp scent.


Merrick came along the deck with his slate, eyes running over the men at
their tasks. He stopped near the foremast, close enough that I could smell
the vinegar on his coat where it had been brushed clean.


"Croft," he said. "You have the foretop this afternoon. Take Kit with you."


Croft nodded without surprise. "Aye, Mister Merrick."


Merrick's gaze moved to me. "You have learned the deck. Now you learn the
height. Do as you are told. Do not look at your feet. If you fall, you will
have wasted everyone's time."


"Aye, sir," I said.


He made a small mark on his slate and moved on. That was all.


Croft hooked a coil of light line over his shoulder and nodded toward the
shrouds.


"Right then," he said. "Time to see if you can climb as well as you scrub."


I swallowed, looking up at the foremast. The top seemed far higher than it
had from the boat that first day. The ratlines made a dark ladder against
the pale sky; the sails above were loosely brailed, catching the breeze in
shallow breaths.


He studied my face a moment, as if weighing something.


"You will not fall," he said. "Not with me there."


I nodded, because it was that or speak and show too much.


"Watch me first," he said. "Then follow."


He dropped the coil of line at the foot of the shrouds and went up in a
smooth, quick run. Hands and feet moved in a rhythm he didn't seem to think
about consciously; left, right, left, right, body close to the lines,
weight moving through his legs more than his arms.


His shirt stretched across his shoulders as he climbed, the cloth drawing
tight where muscle worked. The light caught the damp at the small of his
back, a thin line of darker linen.


I told myself to watch his grip, not his body, but I did a poor job of
it. I prayed silently that he wouldn't distract me when I was in the
rigging.


Halfway up, he glanced down, one hand hooked around a ratline, feet braced
in the shrouds. I looked at the gentle arches of them, toes spread across
the rope, curling, clinging. I was strangely attracted to them.


"Your turn," he called. "Do not stop unless you are told to. Pausing makes
you think too much."


I put my hands on the lowest ratline. The rope felt different from the
holystones' cords: tarred, rough, worn by a hundred hands before mine. My
bare feet found the first line; the mast loomed close to my shoulder. The
ship rolled under me in a way I had not noticed on deck.


"Keep your body in," Croft said. "Close to the shrouds. If you lean back to
look, I will kick you myself."


There were a few quiet laughs from men at the rail. No malice in it, just
the usual pleasure of watching someone new learn.


I went up. One step, then another. The ratlines creaked under my
weight. Rope scraped my palms; my fingers dug deeper. I kept my gaze on the
next handhold and the next, refusing the urge to look between my feet at
the widening strip of water below.


The wind stretched around the mast, cooler than it was on deck. I felt it
catch the sweat at my neck, drying it, raising a little shiver along my
skin.


Croft climbed a few more lines, then held where he was, waiting for me. I
drew level with him at last, breathing harder than I wanted to show.


"Good," he said. "You are not clinging like a cat on a curtain. That is
something."


"High enough for you?" I managed.


"Not yet." His mouth curved in a small smile. "You have higher in you than
this."


He moved again. I followed, closer now, finding the rhythm easier as my
body began to trust the pattern. Once, when the ship rolled a little deeper
to port, my arm brushed his calf. He adjusted without comment, making
space.


When we reached the foretop, he swung himself over the rim with an easy
pull and stood on the platform. I came after, gripping the edge harder than
necessary for a moment before stepping fully onto the wooden circle.


Up there the air felt wilder. The deck below looked smaller, the men on it
reduced to moving shapes. The ship's motion was more pronounced, each slow
sway of mast and yard pulling at my balance.


"Breathe," Croft said, watching my stance. "You are not going to fall."


"I know," I said, though my legs apparently had their own opinion.


He moved closer, not so close as to crowd, but enough that his shoulder was
near mine. His presence did more to steady me than the mast at my back.


"Look out, not down," he said. "There."


He nodded toward the horizon. I forced my eyes away from the ropes and let
them stretch across the line where sky met water. The world opened in front
of me, a broad sweep of gray-blue that made the height feel different, less
like a threat.


Kingfisher cut her path through that space with a confidence I could feel
in my feet.


"There," he said again, softer. "That is why you climb. Not for the
mast. For that."


We stood like that for a moment, side by side, arms nearly touching, the
wind sliding around us. I could feel the heat of him through the thin space
between our sleeves.


He broke it first, nodding toward a bundle of line near the mast.


"Work now," he said. "You can admire the view when Mister Merrick is not
waiting for his braces set a-right."


He stooped to gather up the loose fall of the topsail brace and pressed it
into my hands.


"You know this one," he said. "You felt it on deck. Up here you see what it
answers."


He showed me where it led along the mast and out to the yard. His hand
squeezed mine briefly as he shifted my grip.


"Here," he said. "If it is too slack, she will complain. If it is too
tight, she will complain worse. You want her easy in the wind."


"Like you?" I said before thinking.


The words were out, hanging there between us.


"Do I complain much?" he asked.


"Not where Mister Merrick can hear you," I said.


He let out a short breath that might have been a laugh.


"Pull there," he said. "We'll gab about my complaints another time."


I braced my feet and took the strain as he had taught me. The line
answered, weight travelling through the yard. He watched my hands, my arms,
the set of my shoulders. When I began to brace more with my back than my
legs, he stepped in, close behind me, and set his palm flat against the
middle of my back.


"Bend your knees," he said, voice near my ear now. "Let the lift come from
your legs. You are not wrestling her."


His hand stayed there, warm through the thin linen, while I corrected my
stance. My body reacted on its own, a pull low in my belly that had nothing
to do with the rope. I hoped he could not feel it in the way my breath
shortened.


"There," he murmured. "Better."


He did not move his hand. The ship rolled, and for a moment my weight
shifted back into him, his chest against my shoulder blades, the two of us
aligned along the sway of the mast.


My fingers tightened on the brace. He felt that too. I knew he did, because
I heard the small pause in his breathing.


Then he stepped away, hand leaving my back, and the air rushed in to fill
the space he had occupied.


"That will do," he said, a touch rougher than before. "Now you know how she
moves when you ask her right."


I let the line rest, heart beating faster than the small effort required.


"Not so bad?" he asked.


"No," I said. "Not so bad."


He studied my face for a long moment, wind tugging at the hair that had
come loose from his tie, eyes narrowed slightly against the light. There
was something searching in his gaze now, something weighing risk and
wanting both.


"You did well," he said at last. "First time aloft, most men shake like wet
dogs."


He turned then, stepping out along the yard as if it were the most natural
thing in the world, leaving me with the rope and the gap his body had left
against mine.


"Come on," he called over his shoulder. "You are not done learning. And I
have no wish to explain to Mister Merrick why I wasted such a promising
morning on a man who refuses to move his feet."


I followed, because that was my duty, and because I could not have done
otherwise if I tried.


Croft moved along the yardarm as if he'd been born there. The whole of him
balanced with an ease I couldn't imagine possessing.


I followed more slowly, keeping to the thicker part of the yard. The drop
beneath was a long, sheer fall to the deck and sea. Looking ahead was
easier than looking down. But the sense of height pressed against my ribs
all the same.


"Keep low," Croft said without looking back. "And trust the yard. She won't
throw you unless you insult her."


"How do I insult a yard?" I murmured.


"By remembering how far below your toes the deck is."


He glanced over his shoulder; the grin he gave me was quick, bright, and
entirely too steady for a man balanced forty feet above the deck. It
helped. More than I'd admit.


He stopped at the midpoint of the yard where the lift line hung slack in
the soft breeze. His hand wrapped around it with familiarity, feeling the
tension. He nodded to himself, then crooked a finger at me.


"Here," he said. "Stand here."


I eased toward him, each step a deliberate test of rope, balance, and
courage. The yard was narrower here, barely wider than my foot, but he
didn't move aside as I joined him. Instead, he reached out and caught my
forearm to steady me as the ship rolled under us.


His grip was firm. Warm. Stronger than it needed to be. It sent a pulse
through my skin that the wind did nothing to cool.


"Feel that?" he asked.


"I'm trying not to."


"Feel it," he said again, voice lower. "The mast, the roll, the give in the
yard. Let your knees take it. Don't fight it."


He set his free hand lightly at my hip, guiding me into a bend I wouldn't
have managed on my own. His palm was warm through the fabric of my slops,
fingers braced at the curve of my pelvis.


A shiver went through me. He felt that too. He let me hold that stance a
moment, close enough that I could feel the line of his body along my side
whenever the ship swayed toward us. Then he reached for a length of
buntline, passing it into my hands.


"Take this," he said. "Wrap it twice around your wrist before you lean
out."


"Lean out?"


"Oh aye," he said with a faint spark of mischief. "Else how do you expect
to see what you're doing?"


The yard dipped with the swell. I caught my breath. His hand returned to my
hip, more certain this time.


"Kit," he said quietly. "I won't let you fall."


I wrapped the line around my wrist, fingers clumsy with nerves. He watched,
then stepped in and corrected my grip, his thumb brushing the inside of my
wrist, just above the pulse. The touch was nothing more than instruction,
but my body betrayed me anyway, tightening with a heat I wasn't prepared
for.


He knew. I could see it in his eyes when he lifted them to mine.


"Lean out," he said.


I did. The yard swayed beneath me; the sea opened like a wide blue-brown
mouth far below. My stomach dropped. My back stiffened.


And then Croft's hand slid from my hip to the middle of my back, broad and
warm.


"I have you," he said. "Let go of the fear." The water faded, and instead I
stared into the depths of his eyes.


I breathed out slowly, easing forward, letting the roll guide me. The line
in my hand steadied me more than I expected. Croft's palm steadied me more
than anything else could.


He stood just behind me now, his chest against my back. His breath touched
my neck when the breeze lulled. The space between our bodies closed and
opened with the ship's rhythm, each pass more charged than the last.


"Good lad," he murmured. "You're doing well."


His praise felt like warm tropical air.


I swallowed hard. "What now?" I asked.


"Now you learn to trust your feet more than your eyes."


He stepped around me, moving lightly along the yard until he was just ahead
of me, his back turned. He demonstrated the movement: leaning into the
wind, hauling the line, bracing with his legs instead of his arms.


His shirt clung to his back where sweat or spray had dampened it
earlier. Again I watched the shape beneath it; broad shoulders narrowing to
a lean waist, the flex of his spine, the strength in his hips and thighs.


He noticed when he turned.


His eyes flicked to mine. He didn't look away. Something passed between us
in that sliver of silence, unspoken but unmistakable.


"Your turn," he said softly.


I mimicked his stance. He corrected my feet by nudging them with his
own. He adjusted my shoulders with his hands, fingers pressing briefly into
muscle. Every touch was instructional, but every touch sent tremors through
my body.


The wind caught the sail above us, filling it with a low, deep sound. The
yard dipped again.


Croft reached instinctively, his arm circling me from behind, locking
across my chest as the ship rolled.


For a moment, our bodies pressed flush, his chest at my back, his thigh
against mine, his breath warm and moist at my temple.


"Easy," he said, voice barely above the wind. "I have you."


He didn't release me quickly. I didn't move away. Together we rode the sway
until the mast steadied again.


Only then did he ease his arm back, his palm trailing across my chest
before he let go.


"You're learning fast," he said.


"You're teaching well," I answered, trying to keep my voice even.


His smile this time wasn't a smirk. It was warm and open; genuinely
pleased.


"We'll take a rest," he said. "There's no shame in catching your breath up
here."


He shifted back toward the mast, one hand loose on the stay, waiting for me
to join him. For a moment I didn't move; not from fear, but because the
echo of his body against mine felt too vivid to step away just yet.


The wind teased the loose strands of his hair across his forehead. He
looked at me. "Come on, Kit." His voice was gentle. "I won't have you
trembling up here alone."


I followed him, my breath still uneven, my blood still bright with the
memory of his arm around me, knowing this wasn't just instruction anymore.


And knowing he knew it too.


* * *


At the bottom, Croft dropped down beside me, landing lightly. He gave the
mast an absent pat, then rolled his shoulders once as if shaking off the
height.


Barnes was there at the foot of the shrouds, arms folded, watching us in
that way he had, a combination of sternness and soft concern.


"Well?" he asked. "Looks like the lad lived."


Croft wiped a line of sweat from his brow with the back of his wrist. "He
did more than that."


Barnes's eyes shifted to me. "You feel like you've still got your legs
under you, Kit?"


"Aye," I said. "More or less."


"More or less," he repeated, mouth quirking. "That's better than some. I've
seen men come down from their first time aloft and walk like they've left
their kneecaps in the top."


I could feel Croft looking at me, but I kept my gaze on Barnes.


"It's a long way up," I said. "Feels longer the first time."


"Wait until you go out in a squall," Barnes said. "Then you can tell me
what long feels like."


He reached past me to finger one of the ratlines, testing its tension.


"Mister Merrick will want the fore watch parties sorted this evening," he
went on. "You'll likely be in Croft's." His eyes flicked between us. "Might
as well start as you mean to go on."


Croft tilted his head a little. "You complaining about the company,
Barnes?"


"I have never once complained about competent hands," Barnes said. "Or
about men who make my job easier. Or about men who get the work done
without falling on someone's head." His gaze rested on me again. "You
dragged him back down in one piece, I take it."


"I did not drag him anywhere," Croft said. "He climbed."


"Mm." Barnes's eyes narrowed slightly, the way they did when he caught a
detail others missed. "Still. You look lighter than you did at dawn."


Croft's jaw moved, like he was working out whether the remark was worth
answering.


"Yard's set right," he said instead. "That lightens any man."


"I'm sure that is all it is," Barnes said.


There was no sting in it. Only a dry knowing. I felt heat prick the back of
my neck all the same.


He pushed himself off the shrouds.


"Right," he said. "Since the pair of you are still breathing, you can make
yourselves useful. Gun tackle wants seeing to. Croft, you take him along
the larboard battery. Show him the pattern. Mister Merrick doesn't like his
carriages rigged like a drunk's knitting."


"Aye," Croft said.


Barnes squeezed my shoulder as he passed. "You do what he shows you, Kit,
and you'll live long enough to be sick of the sight of all this."


His hand lingered just long enough to feel like approval. Then he was gone,
striding aft, already watching someone else.


Croft picked up a coil of small line from a rack near the foremast and
jerked his chin toward the main gun deck.


"Come on," he said. "We'll give you a new kind of rope to swear at."


We went below, down the ladder into the gloom where the guns sat in their
ordered rows. The air there was cooler, touched with the smell of old
powder and iron. Narrow beams of light came through the open ports, cutting
across dust and faint spray.


Croft's shoulders filled the space ahead of me as we moved between the
carriages. The height had taken some of the twitch from my legs, but not
all. My senses still felt sharpened, as if the mast had left its mark
running through my bones.


He stopped beside one of the midship guns and dropped the coil onto its
carriage.


"Right," he said. "You've seen these enough from a distance. Time to learn
how they breathe."


I frowned a little. "Guns breathe?"


"When they fire," he said. "And when they recoil. And when they run out
again. Everything that moves has a way of breathing. Man, rope, ship,
gun. Learn that and you'll get in fewer fights with them."


He knelt by the tackle, showing me how the double blocks sat, how the line
reeved through them, how the standing part took its purchase on the
ringbolt in the deck. His fingers worked fast, familiar, the rope slipping
around his palm in a pattern I tried to fix in my mind.


"Here," he said after a moment. "Take this end. Follow me."


He put the free part of the line in my hand, then closed his own around
mine to guide it through the block.


The contact was brief, but not nothing. His skin felt rough from work, yet
warm in a way that had nothing to do with the air.


"Under, through, back around," he said quietly. "Let your hands learn
it. They'll remember quicker than your head."


I followed his movements, the rope rasping against my fingers. Every time I
faltered, he corrected me by touch rather than words, nudging my hand,
adjusting the angle of my wrist. It would have been ordinary if not for the
way my body reacted to each contact, a low pull deep inside that had
nothing to do with guns or war.


We worked like that for another few minutes. The gun deck around us was not
empty, but the men nearby were occupied with their own tasks: polishing
metal, stowing gear, checking breechings. Now and then I felt a glance
drawn our way, curious but not sharp.




Croft moved behind me, reaching past to adjust the set of one of the
blocks, his forearm close along my side. I could feel the warmth of him
even through our shirts. His breath was hot against the nape of my neck as
he leaned in.


"Not there," he said, his voice pitched low for me alone. "You want it
closer to the cheek. You leave too much slack and she'll jump."


I shifted the block as he showed me. The muscles in my back tightened under
the nearness.


"You feel that?" Croft asked.


"The tackle?" I said.


"The line," he said. "Tight enough, but not choking."


I swallowed. "Aye."


"Good," he said. "You learn balance and you'll be worth something on this
deck."


Croft picked up the next coil, dropped it lightly onto the carriage, and
cleared his throat.


I stepped to the next carriage. He followed. We worked. The guns watched us
with blank iron eyes, the ship creaked around us, and somewhere beneath the
sounds of rope and wood and metal, I could hear something else waking up
between us, as real and as risky as any charge of powder.


* * *


The day slipped toward evening. Work eased, the heat broke, and shadows
stretched across the deck. Men gathered in loose groups to mend shirts,
carve bits of wood, or smoke while leaning against the bulwark. The smell
of pitch and salt softened under a cooler breeze.


I came up from the gun deck with the faint ache of new work running through
my shoulders and forearms. Croft wasn't far behind, wiping his hands on a
scrap of cloth, jaw tight in that way he had when he was thinking too much.


I moved toward the rail out of habit, wanting the air and the space. The
sea spread out in a pale-gold sheet, the sun sinking low enough that it
turned every crest of water into a coin. The horizon lay quiet, almost
pressed flat by the fading light.


I set my hands on the rail.  Breath steadied.  The height of the foretop
still lingered in my chest.


Croft came to stand beside me without asking.


He leaned forward on his elbows, gaze on the water. Our sleeves brushed
when the deck rolled; neither of us adjusted.


"Not a bad day for your first run aloft," he said, voice quieter than
before. "Could've been worse."


"I didn't fall," I said.


"No," he said, lips curving faintly. "You didn't."


We watched the sea warming into evening, the breeze brushing our hair and
drying the sweat on our necks. The faint creak of rigging above us made a
soft pattern, almost a companionable sound.


Croft spoke again, softer this time.


"Most men go up the first time with someone who barely looks at them," he
said. "Just tells them where to put their feet, then curses when they get
it wrong."


"You didn't do that," I said.


"No," he said.


A pause settled between us. Not tense. Not empty. Just waiting.


Then he added, more to the horizon than to me, "Didn't want to."


He shifted slightly so that his arm touched mine. I felt the length of his
calf against my leg, skin against skin, the fur of his leg tickling mine.


"You'll be good aloft," he went on. "Better than you think. Takes a
different kind of steadiness up there."


"I didn't feel steady."


"That's because you're new," he said, turning his head just a little toward
me. "Not because you're lacking."


Something eased inside me at that. Something I hadn't realized was pulled
tight.


"You teach well," I said.


"I teach because I see what someone can be before they do," he
said. "Different with every man."


"And with me?" I asked.


His jaw shifted, and he didn't look away from me this time.


"With you," he said slowly, "it's hard not to watch. Hard not to..."


He stopped.


The deck rolled, gentle, and I savored the feeling of him against me in the
cool air. He didn't pull away, and I didn't either.


The sound of laughter drifted from a group near the mainmast; a few hands
playing some game with worn cards, slapping the deck as they argued over
the score. But up here at the rail it felt separate, quieter, as though the
air around us had its own shape.


Croft cleared his throat lightly.


"You'll take to the night watch well," he said. "Cooler. Quieter. Gives you
room to think."


I felt his gaze before I met it; the weight of it, the warmth of it, the
unspoken truth wrapped inside it.


We stood like that, the boards beneath us sighing as the ship breathed with
the tide. The sky had turned from gold to a deeper amber, streaked with
pale clouds. The sun dipped further, sharpening Croft's silhouette beside
me. His profile was all clean lines and weathered strength, the kind you
only gained from years spent on the sea.


The pull between us, so carefully managed all day, stepped forward fully
now.


A voice rose from near the galley. "Dogwatch's up! Anyone wanting supper,
move your feet!"


Croft exhaled slowly, as though releasing something he'd held too long.


He straightened, pushing off the rail, and yet he didn't step away. His
shoulder brushed mine again as he turned slightly toward the line forming
by the mess.


"You hungry?" he asked.


"A bit."


"Then come on," he said, his voice lower, warmer than before. "Else the
Marines will take all the softer biscuits."


He let me walk ahead a step, but only a step. His presence stayed close,
like a shadow moving inside the same breath of space.


The line moved along at a slow crawl, bowls passed forward one by one until
each man had a ladle of whatever the cook had contrived to call
supper. Tonight it was a kind of stew; more water than meat, but it carried
warmth and the taste of salt pork if you chased it down hard enough.


Croft and I took our bowls together. He nodded once toward the space under
the break of the quarterdeck, where the mess tables waited. Men were
already wedging themselves onto benches, boots scraping, shoulders bumping.


We found a gap at a bench midway along. Croft slid in first. I followed,
setting my bowl down, my body aware of the line of him more keenly than I
was of the food. I watched as the top of his shirt parted, revealing the
dark fur and the cleft between the mounds of his chest. He caught me
looking and grinned.


Barnes was at the far end of the same board, talking with a pair of older
seamen. He glanced up as we sat. His eyes passed over the two of us, close
as we were, and something like dry amusement touched his mouth. He did not
comment. For now.


Across from us, a knot of Marines took their places. Their red coats were
hung up by the bulkhead; they sat in shirtsleeves like the rest of us. The
difference showed in their posture more than their clothing: backs
straight, heads high, trained to watch even when they ate.


Rowan Maddox was among them. I recognised him by his build first, then by
the streak of grey at his temple. His face looked as if it had seen too
many long marches and too few soft beds. He spoke quietly to the man beside
him, then his attention shifted and landed, briefly, on me.


It was not the kind of look that undressed or weighed. More like the
assessment of a man who has been told there is a new piece to this small
world and wants to place it properly. His gaze ran from my face to my
shoulders, to the rope-marks still faint along my forearms, then back up.


Croft felt that look even if he did not see it at first. I knew he did,
because the muscle at his jaw moved. His spoon paused halfway to his mouth.


Maddox turned his head a fraction, now taking in Croft as well. Something
unreadable passed through his expression. An acknowledgement. Then he
turned back to his food, conversation resuming on some matter of drill or
guard rotation.


Croft ate in silence for a few moments. His thigh pressed against mine
under the bench, the contact constant, easy, as if we had been sitting this
close for years instead of days. I felt myself leaning into it without
meaning to, drawn by the warmth.


"You're quiet," he said eventually.


"Thinking," I said.


"Dangerous habit," he replied, though there was no real bite to it. "What
about?"


"The height," I said. "The yard. The guns. Maddox."


He flicked a glance across the table. "You know his name already?"


"I listen," I said. "Like you told me."


"He's fair," Croft said. "Hard, but fair. Does not bother men who keep
their hands where they belong."


"And their eyes?" I asked.


He turned his head to me at that. Our faces were close enough that I could
see the flecks of pale blue in his grey eyes, the lines at the corners that
years of squinting into wind and sun had left there.


"Eyes are harder to keep," he said softly.


A lad down the table laughed loudly at some joke, banging his bowl with the
heel of his hand. The sound broke across the mess, then fell back into the
general hum of men eating.


I took a spoonful of stew. It was hot, bland, but it filled the hollow that
the morning's work had carved out inside me.


Across from us, Maddox spoke again, his tone quiet. The Marine beside him,
Broome, gave a short laugh and cut a glance toward our end of the table.


"New lad looks like he'll stand a line," Broome said, not bothering to
lower his voice much.


"New lad is right here," Croft said, without looking up.


Broome raised both hands in a gesture of innocence.


"Didn't say he was deaf," he replied. "Just that he looks sound on his
feet."


Maddox's gaze slid back to me for a moment. He tipped his head a fraction.


"You did well on the ratlines," he said. "Saw you from the waist."


It was not praise the way Barnes or Croft used it. More like a formal
acknowledgement.


"Thank you," I said.


Croft's thigh pressed more firmly against mine under the table.


"You watching the rigging now, Sergeant?" he asked, some dry humour
returning to his tone.


"I watch anything that might fall on my men," Maddox said, unbothered. "Or
on my head. Finials, blocks, topmen."


His eyes moved from Croft's face to the narrow gap between our
shoulders. There was nothing lewd in the observation, nothing mocking. Just
that same steady assessment, the weighing of one more fact in a ship full
of them.


"Kingfisher has carried worse," he said simply. "Far worse than a pair of
hands that climb well."


People got up as they finished, bowls carried back to the galley. The light
outside the ports had darkened to a deepening blue, the first hint of
purple edging the horizon. Lamps were being lit along the bulkheads, the
glow soft and swinging.


Barnes passed behind us, collecting a set of empty tin cups.


"Croft," he said, pausing briefly. "You're on first watch?"


"Aye," Croft said.


He jerked his chin toward me. "And him?"


Croft glanced at me. "With me, if Mister Merrick keeps to his habits."


Barnes gave a single nod. His gaze ran once over the still-narrow gap
between our bodies, then up to our faces. What he saw there must have
satisfied something in him, because his expression softened the smallest
degree.


"Then you'd both best finish and get your rest while you can," he
said. "Night comes quick when you're fool enough to waste the light
talking."


"Good thing we are not fools," Croft said.


Barnes snorted. "That remains to be seen."


Croft watched him go, then looked back at me.


"You hear that?" he said.


"That I should sleep?" I asked.


"That he does not plan to stand between you and anyone," Croft said. "Only
between you and your own carelessness."


"You think I am careless?" I asked.


"No," he said. "I think you might be, if you are not careful where you put
your hands."


His eyes dropped once, briefly, to where our thighs touched under the
bench. The look was quick, but deliberate.


My mouth went dry. I shifted only enough to press more firmly against him,
not less. My hand slid under the table. I don't know what came over me, but
I let my palm grace the mound between his thighs and squeezed gently. I
felt him, thick and meaty. Felt myself flush, and felt his body tense up.


"Yours seem to find their way well enough," I said quietly.


His breath caught; I felt it more than I heard it.


"Sometimes," he said.


We finished the last of our supper in a silence that was not awkward. The
noise of the mess moved around us, loud and ordinary. Men laughed, swore,
elbowed one another in the ribs. From time to time I felt a gaze pass over
us and move on, as if the sight of two men sitting close had already been
catalogued and filed away under the long list of things a ship simply
holds.


When our bowls were empty, Croft stood, stretching his back, the line of
his body long in the lamplight. I didn't think I was imagining the bulge of
him, larger than it was. No else seemed to notice.


"Come on," he said. "There's a bit of time yet before the watch. Enough to
find air."


He did not say rest. He did not say distance.


He offered me the space beside him instead.


I took it.


* * *


By the time the bell marked the first watch, night had taken the ship
entire. The air above had turned cold with the hour, the sea lifting and
settling under Kingfisher with a slow, steady breath. Most of the men had
already gone below; those who lingered did so only long enough to rub
warmth into their hands before swinging down the ladder.


Croft and I descended into the lower deck. Lanterns swayed from the beams,
their light catching on timbers polished by generations of hands. The place
smelled of rope, canvas, salt, and the faint scorch of the lamps. Voices
murmured in low threads, men settling, teasing, complaining, laughing
softly in the half-dark.


The hammocks hung in their ordered rows, each one gently swaying with the
ship's motion. Croft paused by his, his hand resting lightly on the canvas
as if the touch grounded him. I stopped beside mine, only a few paces
away. For a moment neither of us spoke; the quiet held a shape, as though
something we'd spent the day circling had finally taken breath.


He looked at my hammock, then at me.


"You'll feel the cold before long," he said, voice dropping with fatigue
and something warmer. "Wind's turned sharper since dusk."


"It has," I said. "Though I've had worse."


"So have I." His mouth twitched faintly. "Still. There's no sense freezing
for the sake of it."


He didn't say anything more. He didn't have to. The meaning sat plainly
enough between us.


"If you'd rather not sleep alone tonight," he said, more quietly now,
"there's room here. Or there, if you'd prefer I come to you."


"Join me in my hammock, if you would, Mister Croft." I grinned at him
sheepishly. "I'd be glad of some warmth.


He stepped toward my hammock without another word. I caught the edge of it,
lifting the canvas for him. The gesture felt like more than it was.


Croft climbed in with the ease of long practice, the hammock dipping under
his weight. I followed, shifting until our bodies fit along the narrow
width of canvas. The warmth of him seeped through the blanket at once.


"You're certain?" he asked, though his voice already held the answer.


"Aye," I said softly.


The lantern overhead swung slowly, casting gold across his cheek. His eyes
met mine, steady, unguarded, warmer than the watch-lamps above.


He slid an arm beneath my head to adjust my rest. The movement brought our
hips into closer line, his thigh against mine. He did not shift away. The
hammock rocked with the gentle roll of the ship, and his breath warmed the
place just below my ear.


"You kept your feet well today," he murmured. "Up on the yard."


"I nearly lost them," I said.


"Nearly is not the same as falling," he replied. "You did better than most
on their first climb."


The small praise went further inside me than anything Barnes had
said. Croft wasn't a man who offered such things freely.


"You guided me," I said.


"I lent a hand," he corrected. "You did the climbing."


His thumb made a slow, unconscious pass against my waist beneath the
blanket.


"If I guided you," he said after a moment, voice quieter still, "it's
because you trusted the hold."


"I did," I said. "I do."


He drew a slow breath, the kind that sounded more like release than
fatigue.


"It alters a man," he murmured. "Being relied upon."


"How so?"


"It gives the day a different shape," he said. "Sits with a man long after
the light's gone."


I shifted closer, instinct more than intent. He answered in kind, sliding
his hand to the small of my back, then caressing downward over the curve of
my arse. I squirmed against him, encouraging the closeness rather than
shrinking from it.


The deck above groaned softly as the ship leaned into a swell. Lantern
light flickered along the timbered beams. A few men spoke in low voices
nearby, but their talk felt distant, muffled by the small world Croft and I
occupied.


He breathed my name once. "Kit."


"Aye?"


"You'll allow me to stay?" he asked, voice barely above the sway of the
hammock.


"If you'll have me."


He huffed something like quiet amusement. "Seems I will."


His breath touched my jaw then, warm as the blanket, and my chest
tightened.


"You needn't worry what others think," he said. "No man down here sleeps
exactly as he did the first week aboard."


"And you?" I asked.


"I've shared a hammock before," he said. "But it hasn't felt like this."


A shiver ran the length of my spine. I pressed closer, resting my forehead
lightly against his temple.


"Good," I said quietly.


His hand flexed at my back, deliberate now.


For a long while we said nothing more. The ship creaked, the waves
whispered along the hull, the lantern above swung in slow, patient arcs. I
felt the thickness of him against me, this time against my own manhood. I
was sure the both of us could feel our heat through the materials of our
breeches. Croft's breathing grew heavier, drifting toward sleep. The weight
of his arm tightened around me as he sank into it.


The hammock rocked gently, holding us in its narrow cradle as the ship
pressed onward through the dark.


We slept.


* * *
