DRAMATIS PERSONAE


HMS Kingfisher, A sloop-of-war in His Majesty's Royal Navy, beloved for her
spotless decks and the steady hands who keep her true.


Captain Elias Hale - Commanding Officer


Master Merrick - Ship's Master -- Senior Navigator & Deck Authority


Dr. Elias Corbyn - Ship's Surgeon


Able Seaman Thomas Croft


Ordinary Seaman Barnes - Deckhand


Ordinary Seaman Sutter - Deckhand


Corporal Garrick Broome - Royal Marine -- Musketman


Sergeant Rowan Maddox -- Royal Marine Sergeant


James "Kit" Kittridge - Landsman, relatively new to HMS Kingfisher


* * *


CHAPTER 1


I woke before I meant to, the way one does when the ship herself insists
upon it.


The first bell rang somewhere above me, muffled by the beams along the top
of the berth deck. The sound was a thin metallic crack that travelled up
the timbers like a shiver. It pushed through the canvas of my hammock,
through the rope clews at either end, and straight into the bones of my
back. I opened my eyes to the faint curve of the hull overhead. The lantern
at the far end had burned low, throwing only a dim haze over the beams.


I became aware of the warmth at my back. A month of working aboard this
ship and I still had trouble wringing myself awake in these early hours.


The warmth formed into a shape. A body pressed close behind mine with the
unthinking ease of someone who had slept there long enough to settle in.


His name was Able Seaman Thomas Croft, from Dartmouth, in Devon.


His breath touched the back of my neck in small, warm intervals, stirring
the edge of my collar. One of his knees rested just behind my thigh. An arm
lay draped over my belly in the manner of a man who'd surrendered to sleep
without considering whose hammock he'd taken up.


Which was mine.


He'd not been there when I turned in. I remembered that clearly. I'd
climbed into my sling after the second dogwatch, easing into the canvas
while the deck still held the damp of fog coming off the water.


Men spoke in low voices at first, then fell quiet as the lanterns were
doused one by one. I'd lain there listening to the creak of the ship,
trying to imagine the day to come.


Somewhere after that; after the deckhead had gone dark and the murmur of
voices had dulled, Croft had climbed in behind me.


I had learned over the last few weeks that this was not uncommon,
particularly on chilly nights, or when crew rotations made the berthing
deck more crowded than it might otherwise be.


Last night it was nothing but the soft shifting of canvas, a whispered
curse about the cold, and his weight folding into the space beside mine as
if the decision had been made elsewhere.


I hadn't protested then. Really no point in doing that. He meant nothing by
it. And I didn't protest now. The warmth was pleasing and right.


The second bell rang clearer, sharper, carrying with it the rustle of men
stirring. A throat cleared down the row. Someone muttered into a
blanket. Another hammock creaked as its occupant rolled over with a grunt.


I tried easing my hips a fraction to the side to unknot the stiffness in my
back. Croft's arm responded as if roused from its own sleep, tightening
around my middle for the briefest moment before it slackened again. His
hand landed more comfortably just above the waistband of my trousers. The
warmth of his palm soaked straight through the cloth.


I stared hard at the boards above me and willed myself not to think too
closely about it.


The third bell struck; brisk, unmistakable, the kind that meant the day was
beginning whether we liked it or not.


Croft stirred properly at that.


A long breath lifted his chest against my back. His head rose from where
he'd tucked it close, and he made a low sound that might've been a curse,
or might've been his attempt at waking without admitting defeat.


"Morning already," he muttered, voice rough as rope ends. "Christ. They
might've let us lie another quarter-hour."


I kept my gaze fixed upward. "You've commandeered half the hammock as it
is. I didn't think you'd notice the bell."


He gave a quiet, drowsy laugh. "I notice everything, Kit. And you're no
small burden to share a sling with, I'll have you know."


"You're the one who climbed in," I reminded him.


"Aye, well. My own hammock was colder than a magistrate's heart." He
shifted slightly behind me, enough that I felt the smooth brush of his
shirt against my back. "Yours was the better berth."


"You might've asked."


"That'd ruin the charm of it."


A few hammocks over, a man yelped as his feet touched the cold
deck. Someone swore in reply. Croft adjusted himself again, bracing his
hand against the beam above us. His chin brushed the back of my head.


"Right then," he said quietly, clearer now. "Up we go before Broome's lot
trample us on their way to muster."


He began working himself free of the narrow canvas, which is as ungainly as
it sounds when two men have shared it. His thigh pushed against mine, then
away. The ropes creaked sharply. The hammock lurched. Someone across the
row muttered for us to keep quiet if we meant to fall.


"Easy, now," Croft said under his breath, half-laughing as he got one leg
over the edge. "Mind yourself."


He dropped to the deck in a neat, practiced movement, and hissed
immediately as his bare feet found the cold boards.


"God's teeth," he whispered, flexing his toes. Then, with a crooked
half-smile, "Come on, Kit. Mind your step. No shame in a hand, if you need
one."


He offered it; open, simple, unburdened by anything more than practicality,
but I saw the ghost of warmth behind it, the kind that had nothing to do
with cold boards.


I ignored the hand out of pride more than sense and pushed myself upright
using the beam above. My shirt had twisted itself across my chest in the
night. The hammock swung once as I steadied myself, canvas whispering under
my hands.


Croft watched the whole process with unearned amusement.


"See?" he said. "A proper sailor already."


"I'll manage well enough," I answered.


"That's the spirit."


I swung my legs over the edge and dropped. The cold of the planks hit like
a slap. The breath left me at once.


Croft laughed softly.


"Told you," he said. "Colder than a witch's bosom."


He bent to untie the small net slung under his hammock, withdrawing a hard
biscuit that looked as if it had fought several battles of its own. He
broke it with a practiced snap and handed me the larger half.


"Here," he said. "Galley'll be slow by now."


"You're very generous," I said, taking a cautious bite.


"I like my climbing partner to have strength enough to haul himself aloft."
He smirked. "You'd be no use to me fainting in the shrouds."


"You can't call me that."


"What... partner?" He arched his brow. "Would you rather `ballast'? I've
used worse."


"That isn't the comfort you think it is."


"Aye, but it made you answer. That's comfort enough for me."


Men began dropping from hammocks around us in earnest, boots lacing, shirts
tugged over heads, voices rough with sleep. Broome's Marines marched past
with the typical discipline of their lot, boots thudding in even
rhythm. Sergeant Maddox followed at a measured pace, surveying the deck
with the steady calm of a man who saw everything and commented on none of
it.


His gaze passed briefly over us, my shirt still wrinkled from the sling,
Croft with his hair crushed flat on one side, and moved on without pause.


Croft turned toward me slightly. Not enough for anyone else to notice, but
enough that I felt it.


"Right," he murmured. "We're behind the Marines. That's a poor omen for the
day."


He gestured toward the ladder leading up to the main deck. The queue of
seamen was forming already, everyone shuffling forward in the cramped
space.


At the base of the ladder, Croft put his hand briefly at the base of my
back.


"You first," he said. "If you slip, I'll be the one to complain."


"That's meant to reassure me?" I asked.


"Entirely," he said. "You'll fall on something soft."


"If I fall on you, you'll complain for a month."


He grinned. "You're learning."


I climbed.


The air grew cooler the nearer I got to the hatch. The scent of salt and
wind replaced the warm, stale breath of fifty sleeping men. Above, the
square of morning light broadened, gray, not yet bright, but enough to
promise the start of something.


As I stepped onto the main deck, the wind hit my face with a clean
sharpness that woke every part of me that Croft hadn't woken already.


Behind me, I heard him climb the last few rungs. He stepped up beside me.


The ship rolled gently under our feet. The sky above was pale and streaked
with the thinning fog of early light. The deckboards gleamed with a sheen
of moisture. Somewhere forward, a boatswain's whistle shrilled.


"Right then," Croft said, drawing a breath as if he'd been born to this
air. "Let's see what the day means to make of us."


And just like that, the night's closeness was swallowed into the morning.


* * *


By the time we had breathed the morning air, and rubbed the last of the
sleep from our eyes, the sky had shifted from a darker gray to a pale blue,
the kind you only see at sea. The horizon was a thin line, nothing more,
and Kingfisher lay on the water, expectant and ready as she always was.


The deck still held the night's damp. A few hands were already at the pump
in the waist, the open middle stretch of deck between the forecastle and
quarterdeck. I'd mistaken the word, once, with Seaman Barnews, thinking he
was referring to his own body. He'd laughed at that for a day and a half.


The crew moved the long pump handles in a slow, heavy rhythm. Each stroke
brought up a rush of dark water from below, then a spill of it across the
planks. In the half-light, the spray looked almost white.


"Up we come to a fine welcome," Croft said beside me. "Cold water and
work. Navy kindness."


His voice was soft enough not to wake those still coming off watch, but
there was a grin in it.


A boatswain's mate stood near the pump, coil of rope over one shoulder,
silver call hanging at his chest. He nodded once when he saw us.


"You two," he said. "Holystones. We'll have the waist done before Mister
Merrick comes up."


Croft gave a small answering tilt of the head. "Aye."


He stepped away to fetch one of the stones from the stack by the
bulwark. Holystones were worn blocks of sandstone with rope through the
middle. He tossed another toward me; I caught it with both hands, arms
protesting after a night in the sling.


The mate jerked his chin toward the wet planks. "On your knees,
then. Sand's there. Make it shine."


Croft dropped easily, the motion fluid as if he'd been born to it. His
trousers darkened at once with the damp. The linen of his shirt clung
across his shoulders where it brushed the wet; under it, his back moved in
long, even lines as he pushed the stone forward over the sand.


I followed him down. The deck was cold enough to bite through skin. When I
put my hands flat on the boards, the grain felt rough and swollen with
night water. A few flakes of dried salt clung to the seams.


We set the stones to work side by side, arms rising and falling in the same
rhythm. It was a miserable sort of task if you thought about it too much,
grit grinding into the cuts on your fingers, water soaking up through your
knees, the ache in your shoulders and back.


But there was a satisfaction to it too, seeing the boards grow lighter in
our wake, feeling the ship answer under us as if she approved, like a dog
leaning into the scratching between his shoulders where he couldn't reach.


The pump handles squeaked, up and down, up and down. Each time the bucket
filled, one of the pumpmen swung it forward in an easy half-arc, sending
the water across the part we had just scrubbed. It chased the sand and soap
toward the scuppers in little rivers.


One of those splashes broke over my hands and ran up my forearms; it was
colder than I expected, clean in a way river water never was. The chill
sank into my bones and, before I could stop it, a memory rose with it, as
if the water had washed it loose.


I recalled that first day. That same pump. The same cold.


I slowed without meaning to, the stone dragging a shorter arc. Croft
noticed at once. Of course he did. He had a talent for catching the way a
man's rhythm changed.


"You've gone quiet," he said, still working, not looking at me yet. "More
than is usual for you."


The boards in front of me blurred for a moment. The sound of the pump grew
louder in my ears. The smell of salt sharpened.


"Beg pardon, Mister Croft. I was thinking," I said.


"That explains the danger in your eyes."


I snorted despite myself. "You remember my first day?"


A pause; only a short one, but it sat there.


"You think I would forget it?" he said at last.


The next bucket went over with a harsh splash, the water slapping into the
same groove on the deck that had caught me that first morning, clean broke,
road-worn, wearing every mile from London to Portsmouth on my back.


The deck in front of me shifted, and I was there again.


The boat that carried me out to Kingfisher had shipped more water than
comfort. I remember that as clearly as anything, the way the river's chop
slapped the bow, the way the spray caught the front of my coat, the way the
coxswain's jaw never seemed to move as he cursed the tide.


She had looked enormous from that low vantage. Not in the way of the great
ships that loomed like floating towns, but in a more focused way; every
line tight and beautiful, every rope in its place. The hull rose above us
clean and dark, paint well cared-for. Her gunports were shut, lids tight,
like a row of eyes keeping their own counsel. The figurehead at her bow,
the little bird carved in flight, seemed to be looking somewhere beyond us
entirely.


"HMS Kingfisher," the older hand beside me said, when he caught me
staring. "You could do worse, lad."


I did not yet know what that meant, but I would eventually.


The boat-hook caught the chains and held us there, bobbing gently against
her side. The rope ladder dropped down; it twisted a little with the
movement of the water. The coxswain jerked his chin upward.


"Up you go," he announced. "Unless you'd rather spend the watch out here."


My hands had already been numb from the cold, but I put them on the wet
rope anyway. It felt slimy with weed where the tide kissed it. My boots
slipped once, catching on the rung below. The ladder swayed; I pressed
close into the hull, feeling the rough planks scrape my shoulder through
the coat.


Above me, a head appeared at the entry port: a seaman with his hair tied
back, eyes narrowed against the dim. He watched me come up with half an
interest, as if he'd seen a hundred lads do it worse.


By the time I reached the opening, my arms burned. Someone caught a grip on
the back of my coat and hauled me up, not un-gently, and I found myself
stumbling onto Kingfisher's deck.


It seemed larger beneath my feet than it had from the boat. The planks ran
away fore and aft, worn smooth where men's boots had marked the years into
them. The smell was different too: less of mud, more of pitch and salt and
something sharper I would later learn to be vinegar and camphor. The wind
brought a hint of open water beyond the river.


"Over here," a voice said.


I followed it toward the mainmast, where a small group of men were
gathered; a few other new hands, their eyes wide, and some older ones who
had the look of those who had seen it all before. Beside them stood a man
with a slate under his arm and chalk in one hand: Mr Merrick, the ship's
master, though I did not yet know his name.


He had a lean face that looked as if it had been shaped by years of
watching the horizon: eyes set to distance, mouth at rest unless given good
reason to move. His coat was well kept; his shoes were polished even at
that hour.


"Name," he said without preamble.


"James Kittridge," I replied.


"From where?"


"London," I said.


He regarded me for a moment, as if weighing me up. Coat, boots, the bag at
my side, the way I stood squarely on my feet.


"Very good, Kittridge. You do as you're told and things will go well for
you here."


"Yes, sir."


"`Aye' will do."


"Aye," I corrected.


That earned the faintest shift at the corner of his mouth. Another man
might have missed it. I did not.


He turned his head a fraction toward the quarterdeck. Captain Hale was
there, hat under one arm, one hand resting lightly on the rail. He looked
as if he had been born on that particular plank and never felt the need to
leave it. His gaze passed over the lot of us, new hands huddled together,
and settled for a breath on me.


I felt it land and move on. There was no censure in it, but no welcome
either.


"Fresh from the road," Merrick said, more to himself than to anyone
else. "Smell of stables and cheap ale."


I flushed, though I knew he was right. I'd just turned 18 and hadn't yet
learned how to handle the amount of ale an older man might.


He did not say it cruelly. He shifted the slate under his arm and spoke a
little louder.


"Barnes," he called.


A man detached himself from a working party near the mainmast: thick
through the shoulders, hair tied back, jaw dark with a day's worth of
beard. He walked with the easy balance of someone whose feet knew every
motion the deck could make.


"Aye, Mister Merrick," he said.


Merrick tipped his head in my direction.


"Take this one," he said. "Wash him. Slops from the chest near the
pump. I'll not have the captain's deck taking on half of London at his
heels."


Barnes's eyes ran over me once, quick and practical.


"Aye, sir," he said. "We'll put a shine on him."


Merrick's gaze shifted past him.


"Croft," he said. "Lend a hand. And mind you use soap, not river water and
good intentions."


"Aye, Mister Merrick."


That voice had come from behind me and to the side. When I turned, I saw
him properly for the first time.


He was younger than Barnes by a few years, but older than me by enough to
show it. His hair was black, but a little sun-faded where it curled at his
temples, long, but not burdensome, tied back at his neck with a bit of
cord. Stubble lay light along his jaw, not yet heavy enough to call a
beard. He wore his shirt open at the throat; a line of dark hair traced
down from there and disappeared under the linen.


He carried a coil of line in one hand, which he dropped easily onto a cleat
before crossing the deck toward us. His eyes were pale, the sort of gray
that could pass for blue in the right light. They took me in from head to
foot.


If there was anything more than assessment in it, I was not yet used to
recognising it.


"Right," Barnes said. "You heard the man. Come on."


He took me by the elbow in a way that did not admit argument, though he
didn't grip hard. I wasn't a prisoner, after all. I picked up my bag. Croft
stepped in on my other side.


"Coat too," Barnes had said. "No sense washing one and not the other."


"I can carry it," I said.


"I've no doubt," he replied. "But you'll not have a hand for it once you're
under the bucket."


We walked aft into the waist, toward the pump. The sky above was much as it
was now: thin, washed-out light with the sun still somewhere behind a veil
of cloud. The air had that particular chill that seems to live nowhere else
but on the water at dawn.


The pump stood with its handles idle, two buckets beneath the spout. The
planks round it were bare of sand but stained pale from constant
scrubbing. A few men leaned on the rail nearby, finishing their own washing
or talking low.


Barnes set my bag by the bulwark.


"Right," he said, businesslike. "You'll strip. All of it. Shirt, breeches,
the lot. Keep your things together; you'll want them dry if we make a gift
of you back to the land one day."


My hands found the buttons on my coat without quite feeling them. I could
feel eyes on me, though no one came close. It was not the sharp curl of
mockery I knew too well from London yards; more like the curiosity of men
watching a new rope being hauled up, wanting to see if it would hold.


Croft moved past me to the pump. He set his hands to the handle and worked
it, arm and shoulder flexing under the linen as the mechanism coughed and
then gave up water. He looked over once, saw me fumbling with a cuff, and
his mouth twitched.


"You'll freeze if you take all morning about it," he said. "Best be done
quick, lad."


There was no laughter in it, not really. A hint of it, maybe. Enough to
save me from feeling like a carcass in a market.


I shrugged off my coat and folded it, setting it atop the bag. The
waistcoat followed, then my shirt. The air rushed at my bare skin with the
eagerness of something that had been waiting. I tried not to shiver and
failed.


Barnes's eyes flicked across my chest and shoulders, not lewd, simply
taking stock, the muscle laid in from work on shore, the faint lines of old
scrapes and knocks, the blond hair that ran down my torso and arms.


"You'll do," he said. "No one will accuse you of being underfed."


I did not know if it was praise, but I counted it as something close and
held onto it.


"Boots," he added.


By the time I had them off, Croft had filled the bucket. He lifted it as if
it weighed nothing, watching me through the thin curls of breath that rose
from both our mouths in the cold.


"Stand there," he said, nodding to a bare patch of plank near the
scupper. "Feet apart, hands to the rail. Else you'll jump and crack your
skull."


I did as he said. The wood under my feet felt harder without the boots,
every ridge of grain pressing into my soles.


"Ready?" he asked.


"Will it matter?" I said.


"No," he replied.


The bucket came over me in one hard sweep. The water crashed across the
back of my head and neck, then poured down my spine in a sheet. It stole my
breath so cleanly I could not even swear. My hands tightened on the
rail. My eyes stung.


I heard someone chuckle near the pump; another voice said, "We've all had
it, he'll live."


"Good," Barnes said. "Soap."


He passed me a chunk of rough gray soap. I turned it over in my hand; it
smelled faintly of lye and something like tallow. I worked it into a lather
as best I could and ran it over my arms, chest, ribs. The road came off in
streaks I had not realised were there.


"Back," Barnes said after a moment. "You'll not manage it alone."


I hesitated, just for a heartbeat.


Croft stepped closer. "I've got him," he said.


He took the soap from my hand, his fingers brushing mine only briefly. He
moved behind me; I felt the heat of him before his hands touched my skin.


"Stand easy," he said, quieter now. "You're not being hanged."


His palm came to rest between my shoulders, firm, and the soap followed. He
worked it over my back with steady pressure, fingers spreading to reach
either side of the spine. The roughness of the soap and the firmness of his
hand set a peculiar sensation going in me; half comfort, half something I
did not care to name.


I stared straight ahead at the line of the rail, breathing through my
mouth. Each time his hand slid down and back up again, his thumb brushed
nearer to the small of my back. Not in a way that could be called improper;
just close enough that my body took notice.


"You carried that bag a long way," he said. "You've knots all through
here."


"I managed," I said. It came out tighter than I intended.


"I can feel that too," he replied.


Barnes watched us with that same half-amused eye.


"Never seen you so attentive, Croft," he said. "You planning to present him
to the captain yourself?"


"Mister Merrick asked for soap," Croft said, not missing a stroke. "I'll
not be blamed for a patch left unwashed."


"The captain will sleep easy knowing the boy's spine is spotless," Barnes
said.


Croft's hand slowed a moment, just enough for me to feel the difference. I
could not see his face, but I heard something in his voice when he
answered.


"He's on our deck now," he said. "Best he starts clean."


It should not have meant anything. It did. The words lodged under my ribs,
even colder than the water and somehow warmer than his hand.


He rinsed the soap away with a second bucket, thrown with more care than
the first. The water was no less cold, but it felt different for coming
from him. When it was done, he ran his hand down once more, the last of the
suds chasing his fingers.


"Turn," he said.


I turned. The air struck front rather than back. For half a breath we were
closer than was required, his face only a hand's breadth from mine. His
eyes flicked down over my chest then up again, quick as a gull's wing, but
he did not pretend he had not done it.


Up close like that, I saw that his lashes were fairer than his hair. There
was a tiny pale scar at one corner of his mouth; it pulled a little when he
smirked, though he was not smiling now.


"You'll pass," he said. "But mind you wash below." He handed me the soap,
and I made good use of it, scrubbing between my legs, and between the twin
globes of my backside, where uncleanliness tended to gather.


Another rinse of the frigid water, and I was at last as clean as I was
going to get. My blond hair hung wet over my eyes, and I reached up to
brush the locks back over my ears.


Croft watched me. It was nothing. It was everything.


Barnes tossed the slops from the chest near the bulkhead: a shirt, a pair
of trousers, both stiff from drying but clean. I shrugged into the shirt
with fingers that did not quite want to obey, the coarse linen scraping
over skin still chilled and stinging. The trousers hung a little loose on
my hips until Barnes reached in and tugged the cord tight, knotting it with
practised speed.


"There," he said. "You look more like a seaman and less like someone who
lost his way out of a city lane."


"Boots later," Croft added. "Barefoot till you learn how she moves under
you. Less chance you go skating into a gun."


I glanced down at my feet. They looked oddly naked on the deck, toes red
from cold. The boards felt different already: less like an unfamiliar
floor, more like something alive.


Merrick came down the deck a few minutes later, slate still tucked under
his arm. He examined me once, head to toe, no more expression on his face
than before.


"Better," he said. "I see you've found the soap, Croft."


"Aye, Mister Merrick," Croft said.


"Kingfisher runs clean," Merrick went on. "Remember that, Kit. Put your
mark on a rope if you must, not on the planks." I remember being surprised
that my nickname had already found its way to someone's tongue here. Kit is
what I had always been called in London. Maybe I simply looked like a
"Kit".


"Aye, sir," I said.


He nodded, already looking away. "Barnes, show him his hook. Croft, you're
back to your party. Deck won't sort itself."


"Aye, sir."


Barnes gripped my shoulder, an anchor of a hand, then guided me forward
toward the ladder that would take me to the berth deck


As we turned away, I looked back once. Croft was already at the pump again,
working the handle with another hand, shirt sticking to his back where
water had splashed him. He did not look over; there was no reason he
should. Even so, a part of me wanted him to.


I followed Barnes down into the dim, my skin still humming with cold and
touch.


Back in the present, a wave slapped Kingfisher's hull and the pump
squealed, dragging me back into that early dawn.


The waist looked different now that I had been here for weeks instead of
minutes; more familiar, less like the stage of an examination and more like
another piece of the ship's body. The planks beneath my knees were the same
though, and the pump's song had not changed.


Croft had stopped pushing his stone. He was watching me, elbow resting
lightly on his knee, rope of the holystone wrapped in his hand.


"You were there," he said quietly. "In your head."


"Aye," I said. "Back at the pump."


He huffed a soft breath that might have been amusement. "Hard thing to
forget, that first wash. Isn't it."


"You took your time about it," I said, more dryly than I felt.


His eyes narrowed just a fraction.


"Would you have preferred I hurried?" he asked.


"No," I said, before I could swallow the word. "I suppose I would not."


That earned a real smile, small but unmistakable. The faint scar by his
mouth caught it.


"Then I make no apology," he said. "You looked like you'd come a long way;
seemed right you should know someone had his hand at your back."


"You were doing as you were told," I managed.


"There are ways of doing as one is told," he answered. "You'll learn that
too."


Before I could ask what he meant, another bucket came across the deck,
catching his sleeve. His shirt went dark and sheer where it clung to his
arm, the muscle filling the fabric. He shook some of the water off with a
quick flick, then pushed the hair back from his face with the heel of his
hand.


Barnes's voice came from near the pump.


"Stones don't move themselves," he said. "Mister Merrick will have our
hides if he comes up to a filthy deck."


Croft did not look away from me at once. He let the silence hold for a
heartbeat longer than was polite.


Then he bent back to work.


"Come on, Kit," he said, rope between his fingers again. "We'll have this
shining before the sun decides whether it's worth the trouble of showing
itself."


I set my hands to the stone once more. The grit bit into my palms. The cold
crawled up my shins. Somewhere forward, a gull cried, its thin voice
carrying over the water.


As I pushed the stone in long arcs, side by side with him, the memory
settled into place alongside the morning; two layers of the same deck: one
with me raw and shivering under his hands, the other with me kneeling in my
slops, moving with him as if we had done this always.


I had not known, that first day, what any of it meant.


I barely knew now.


But as the dawn light slowly took hold of the sky and Kingfisher came fully
awake around us, I carried the knowledge quietly, from the very beginning,
before I knew his name, before I knew the feel of his weight in a hammock
beside me, there had been his hand at my back, and his eyes on me, and the
cold clean bite of salt water tying us both to this ship and to each other,
whether either of us chose it or not.


* * *


When the decking in the waist was finally scrubbed pale, Barnes dismissed
us both with a jerk of his chin and muttered "Mister Merrick will be
satisfied." The crew dispersed toward the morning's assignments, shuffling
into their tasks with the dull focus of men who have done the same work a
hundred mornings before.


Croft wiped a hand across his jaw, flicking water from the edge of his
sleeve. "Come on," he said. "You've learned the stone. Now you need the
lines."


I followed him to the starboard rail where coils of rope hung in ordered
ranks, each one marked with twists or tarred ends to show its purpose. The
dawn had strengthened by then, a band of pale gold cutting across the sky
above the horizon. The wind touched the cloth of my shirt and raised a
chill along my arms.


Croft reached for a coil overhead, lowering it with a pull that showed the
muscle in his forearm flexing under the damp linen. Bless me, I couldn't
stop noticing these things.


He set the rope on the rail between us, fingers running along its length
with a familiarity that reminded me of a man handling a blade he trusted.


"This," he said, tapping the rope, "is the starboard tops'l brace. You'll
be hauling it often. Best you learn the feel of it before someone shouts at
you in the middle of the night."


I nodded, though I wasn't sure what I was meant to feel for yet.


He caught the uncertainty. "Here," he said. "Give me your hand."


My breath caught at that; not enough for anyone else to hear, but enough
for me to feel it. He didn't wait for my answer. He took my right hand in
his left, turning it palm-up, placing it on the rope.


His hand was warm. Mine wasn't.


"You see this lay?" he said, guiding my fingers along the twisted
strands. "Follow it. Don't fight it. Rope works with you if you learn its
grain. Like wood. Or water."


His thumb brushed the inside of my wrist as he shifted my grip. I hoped to
God he didn't feel the pulse that kicked there.


I swallowed. "Feels rougher than I expected."


He snorted something like a laugh. "It'll take hide off you the first
week. Everyone thinks the sea will be all wind and salt and romance. It's
mostly this. Rope, splinters, sweat. And shouting."


"How long until the hands stop bleeding?"


"When you stop holding on like a priest clutching his last sermon," he
said. "Relax. Grip it like this."


He moved closer behind me, not touching fully, just enough that I felt the
heat of him along my back, the light pressure of his chest brushing my
shoulder as he reached around to adjust the angle of my arm. His breath
stirred the hair at the side of my neck.


I looked straight ahead. The sky. The water. Anything but him.


"There," he murmured. "Let the weight fall through your legs, not your
arms."


He stepped back, leaving the ghost of his warmth behind. My body missed it
more quickly than I cared to admit.


I pulled on the rope as he showed me. The weight shifted in my hand in a
way that made sense at last.


"That's it," he said. "Now again."


We repeated it. Once. Twice. A dozen times. Each time he corrected
something small; a tightening of my shoulder, a flare of my elbow. The kind
of adjustments a man could shout across a deck, but he didn't. He came
close instead, quiet, precise. No performance. No swagger.


It felt like the ship around us blurred for a moment, the sounds of the
morning fading while he watched me pull, the corners of his mouth softening
with a kind of approval I had never sought but wanted anyway.


Barnes called something from the mainmast, an order I didn't catch, and
Croft glanced over his shoulder.


"You'll learn faster than most," he said.


"You sound certain."


"I am." His eyes flicked down, briefly, to my forearms, where rope-burn
from the scrub had raised faint red marks. "You work hard. That counts for
more than knowing how to swear like the rest of us."


I smirked. "I can swear well enough."


"I don't doubt it." His gaze lifted again, meeting mine. "But you
listen. That's rarer."


He coiled the brace and hooked it up again. The deck bell rang once
forward. The day had begun properly.


Croft straightened, wiping his hands on his trousers.


"I'll be aloft soon," he said, nodding to the rigging. "You're not there
yet. But you will be."


"More rope first?" I asked.


He studied me a moment, his expression unreadable in the growing light,
then nodded once.


"Aye," he said. "More rope first."


He started toward the next coil, then paused and looked back, something
unreadable flickering in his eyes.


"Stay close," he said quietly. "You're learning well."


And then he moved on.


* * *


The bell struck the half-hour, and the deck changed with it. Men peeled off
into their work parties; Marines filed aft to begin their drills. The
ship's motion had settled into a long, predictable roll, as if the
Kingfisher herself were waking fully now that the sun had begun to clear
the mist.


Barnes strode past Croft and me, a coil of small line over one shoulder.


"You two," he said, nodding at us. "You'll take the bowlines on the
forecourse. Mister Merrick wants them checked and made fast before the
watch changes."


Croft nodded. "Aye."


I followed him forward toward the foremast. The deck narrowed slightly
there, and the bow lifted and fell with a more eager motion than
amidships. The air tasted sharper, the sea closer.


Croft leaned his weight into the rail for a moment, watching the swell
beneath. His hair, still damp from the pump, caught a glint of pale
light. Then he straightened, reached for the bowline lying coiled near the
belaying pins, and began to pull it free.


"You've handled smaller ropes already today," he said. "This one's
heavier. You'll feel it."


I took the line when he offered it. He was right -- it weighed more than I
expected. The roughness dug into my palms.


Croft watched the way my hands settled on the rope.


"You're gripping too high," he said.


"How far down should I--?"


"Here."


He stepped in close, sliding his hand down mine until our fingers nearly
touched. The warmth of him pressed along my forearm as he guided the line
into the proper lay in my hands.


"There," he murmured. "You want the pull to land here..."


His fingers tapped lightly against the middle of my palm, the contact small
but certain.


"Not in your fingertips. Else you'll tear yourself raw."


His eyes flicked to mine, brief as a match-strike, and a quiet
understanding passing between us like something too fragile to name just
yet.


"Now put your weight into it," he said, his voice back to neutral, though
something underneath it had changed; a softness, or maybe a kind of
interest he wasn't bothering to hide fully anymore.


I pulled. The bowline moved, heavy but obedient.


Croft nodded once, approving.


"Good. Again."


We worked like that for several minutes; me hauling, him adjusting the
tension, stepping in when I faltered, correcting my grip by touch instead
of instruction. Each time he reached for the rope, our hands touched.


A gust caught the forecourse high above us and sent the line singing. I
staggered a pace to the side, caught off guard. Croft moved fast -- his
hand shot out, catching me by the upper arm before I could stumble into the
pin-rail.


His grip was firm, fingers circling the muscle just above my elbow.


"Easy," he said. "She's just testing you."


I steadied myself. His hand didn't drop at once. It stayed, warm through
the linen, the pressure of it unmistakably protective. And something
else...


Then, as if he realized it himself, he released me, but not before his
thumb made a small stroke against the inside of my arm, a gesture so
fleeting I almost wondered if it had really happened.


"You all right?" he asked.


"Aye," I managed. "Just slipped."


"You'll find your sea legs soon enough." A pause. And then, lower, "You're
not hard to catch."


I looked at him sharply.


He smiled. It was just enough to show the faint scar near the corner of his
mouth and the warmth that had begun to settle behind his eyes.


Before I could answer, Barnes called from amidships.


"Croft! Kit! If you're done flirting with the wind, get those bowlines made
up!"


Croft didn't turn to look at him. His smile deepened a fraction.


"We're making them up," he called back.


Barnes grunted something unintelligible and moved on.


Croft bent to the line again, but this time he didn't hide the small
grin. I felt my face warm and hoped the dawn light hid it.


When the last knot was tied and the rope made fast, Croft rested his hand
briefly on my shoulder; the barest press of palm to say something without
speaking it aloud.


"You did well," he said quietly. "More than well."


I swallowed. "Thank you for showing me."


"Aye," he answered. "You listened. That's the thing that matters."


The deck bell struck again, calling the watch to change.


Croft stepped back, eyes lingering. "Come on," he said. "Time for
breakfast."


* * *


By the time Croft and I made our way aft, the sun had climbed enough to
break properly through the clouds. The light hit the deck in warm stripes
now, catching on brass and wet rope, turning the ship into something
brighter than she had been at dawn.


Hands were drifting toward the mess tables, bowls and tin cups in their
fists. The smell of porridge and ship's biscuit drifted with the wind,
nothing fine, but welcome all the same.


Croft walked just ahead of me, and the deck pitched underfoot. Something
about the morning; the work, the memory, the rope between us, had settled
in his stance. Or maybe it had settled in mine.


Barnes passed us, carrying a bucket of fresh water. He gave Croft a
sideways look, then me, then a grunt that sounded suspiciously like
amusement.


"Try not to trip over each other," he said without slowing.


Croft didn't rise to it. He just kept walking, that faint curl at the
corner of his mouth returning.


We reached the mess area under the break of the quarterdeck. A few early
eaters had already claimed spots. Croft nodded to a space against the
bulkhead.


"There," he said. "Close enough to pass the time without listening to every
fool's argument about the weather."


I sat across from him, bowl in hand. The porridge was watery, the biscuit
hard enough to crack a tooth, but the warmth of the food settled something
in my stomach that the cold of the dawn had left knotted.


Croft ate with an easy economy, the way a man does when the day is only
beginning its demands. But his eyes lifted now and then, catching mine
across the rim of his cup.


"You'll sleep well tonight," he said between bites.


"More than last night," I said before I could think better of it.


His gaze held mine a moment longer than the words required, amused and dark
with something I couldn't quite place.


He scraped the last of his porridge with the side of the spoon, then set
the bowl aside.


"Work gets easier," he said. "Ship gets smaller. Men get less imposing."


I looked down at my hands, still a little raw across the knuckles despite
the washing, still smelling faintly of tar and soap.


"Do I look frightened?" I asked.


"No," he said. "Not frightened. Just... new."


His words weren't unkind. He leaned back a little, bracing one arm on his
knee.


"You'll grow into her," he went on. "Kingfisher. And the work. And the
watch. And..." His eyes flicked down, then up again. "Everything else."


I felt heat rise beneath my shirt, a warmth entirely different from the
porridge.


"What about you?" I asked quietly.


He blinked once, slowly. A small smirk formed.


"Me?" he said. "Oh, I'll get used to you in time."


A whistle cut across the deck; the call for the next work party.


Croft stood, rolling his sleeves to the elbow, forearms bare and glistening
a little from the morning's work.


"Come on, Kit," he said, voice steady, warm. "We've more to do."


I rose and followed him into the brightening day, the boards solid beneath
my feet, the ship breathing around me, the line between us stretched taut
and certain as any rope aboard the Kingfisher.


I watched him from behind, the broadness of his shoulders, the narrowness
of his waist, the muscles cording and flexing under his shirt. God help
me. I put it down, stashed it away. There was work to be done, after all.


Still, I watched.


* * *
